>From stevec@violet.berkeley.edu  Thu Jan 20 09:25:55 1994
From: stevec@violet.berkeley.edu (Steve Collins)
Subject: Hi, I'm back -- and F&F
To: sfraves@techno.Stanford.EDU
Date: Thu, 20 Jan 1994 09:25:54 -0800 (PST)

We probably don't need another post on F&F, but I felt a need to say
something.

If you don't know me, hi, I'm Stevie.  That's what I'm called in rave land
mostly, anyway, you can also call me Steve or Steven if you prefer.  I used
to be around a lot more than I have been the past few months, but what with
finding a major I like and really diving into academics, getting involved in
a serious relationship, and moving into an apartment together, going out got
lost in the shuffle most of the time.

Hopefully that'll be changing.  Because I had a *great* time at
Friends&Family.  I didn't even really dance that much (I have a hard time
getting into a groove when it's so crowded, but that's just me) but just
being with all the "gang" - Brian B, Laura, Susie, Marlo, Cin, Jo, Mykl,
Steve, everyone - made me realize a coupla things.  First, these are all
really wonderful people and I shouldn't neglect them.  I would benefit
greatly by spending more time around them.  Second, if I got around and met
some more of the people at these parties, I would find even *more* wonderful
people and I would run the risk of being asphyxiated by wonderfulosity,
which probably wouldn't be so bad.

'S funny, because I actually have known Steve for a while (met him at the
same party I met my sig O, matter o' fact) but I didn't know that he'd moved
to SF, changed his net-name to resonate, gotten so involved in sfraves, or
that the Blue Cube was his house until I got there.  Small world.

The thing about the party (this message loses point on cohesiveness and
flow, I know) was that it was a really comfortable place.  Sometimes even at
parties where I know a lot of people I don't really feel like I "fit in"
entirely.  I guess I'm still a little shy; I sometimes feel like a little
kid or a goofball at raves.  Not sure why.  But at any rate, I didn't feel
like that at F&F.  That has a little to do with me growing up a bit, but it
has a lot to do with the party-throwers maintaining a good atmosphere, and
discouraging people from "looking down their noses" as people have done at
some of the parties I've been to.  I saw that Steve made an effort to go
around and try to talk to all the people there and let everyone know what it
was all about.

The point is that I'm hoping to get more involved.  I don't have the time to
get as involved as some people here do; I'm glad to help out some setting up
and tearing down parties, but I can't pretend to have the motivation to put
in the effort that people like Brian, Bryce, Ethan, Steve, Lee, and Geoff
have done, much as I might like to.  But I'll try to do my part to feed the
vibe.  It might be hard, because I am extremely serious about my studies,
and also unfortunately my sig O Jolie likes raves not at all, and I don't
want her to be a "rave widow" but I'll try to make it out as much as I can.

You know, though, there is one way I might be able to contribute - I'm doing
independent study at the Center for New Music and Audio Technologies.  In
computer and electronic music at the research level, there is some crazy
incredible avant-garde type shit going down, very little of which is
trickling down to techno and house music.  I'd like to see if it's possible
to apply some of this stuff in a form you can still dance to.  Two things
that immediately come to mind are first, the manipulation of MIDI data.
This is the concept that when you hit a key on the keyboard, you don't have
to just play that note, but the fact that you pressed it and how long and
how hard you pressed it can be used as just one element of a data stream
that can be manipulated by random or not-so-random processes to create
music seemingly totally different.  Second, the idea of alternative
controllers.  Instead of just plinking on a keyboard, one can play a MIDI
guitar (and as someone who likes distorted-guitar rock, the idea of mixing
the sound of the direct guitar output with the synthesized MIDI is
intriguing), slide controllers, wands that you can wave around and bang
against things, and gloves that you can use to make music with gestures.  I
like the idea of constructing big huge cranks or levers or whatever that you
have to put a lot of physical effort into to get MIDI data out of.  I don't
know if these or any other projects could really pan out, but it's a
thought.  ANy comments?

Well, sorry this is so long, but I wanted to voice and extended Hello Again.

thanks for reading,
stevie

From sfraves-owner  Thu Jan 20 15:30:24 1994
From: mcg@netcom.com (MykL G)
Date: Thu, 20 Jan 1994 00:44:55 GMT
Subject: UNIX Support Position, WIRED Magazine
Status: 

Hi, Everyone...  I've been asked to forward this job listing that's been
posted to ba.jobs.offered specifically to SFRaves for your consideration.
There are already four of us working in this group in various capacities,
so if you're qualified, by all means rush us your resume right away!

Deadline is this coming Monday morning!

--------------------

WIRED, a San Francisco based, internationally distributed magazine focusing
on the digital revolution is looking for a UNIX support person to assist
in our rapidly expanding Online group.  Responsibilities include:
 
*  Performing backup and maintenance of all UNIX machines
   (SGI IRIX and BSD/386).
 
*  Managing related network hardware, including Ethernet, LocalTalk, ISDN,
   and T1 networks, hubs, and routers, as well as modems and
   modem-concentrators.
 
*  Managing all UNIX user accounts.
 
*  Keeping all UNIX software current.
 
*  Taking responsibility for UNIX system security.
 
*  Configuring and administering electronic mail gateways and other TCP/IP
   based UNIX network services.
 
*  Assisting in setup, configuration, and maintenance of UNIX-based server
   software, including email, Gopher, and WWW servers.
 
*  Assisting the overall System Administrator and the rest of the Online
   Services group with UNIX related problems.
 
Ideal candidates will have direct experience with most or all of the
above tasks.  Extra consideration will be given to candidates having
experience with Macintosh hardware, software, and network administration.
 
Candidate must currently live in SF Bay Area.
 
The position will initially be a low-paying, 30-40 hour / week internship,
lasting approximately three months.
 
Deadline for receipt of resumes is Monday, Jan 24th at 9 AM PST.
 
Please send your resume in ASCII-only (no attachments, PostScript or
Acrobat documents, or binary formats, please; URL's are acceptable and
encouraged) to: 
 
   online-nerd@wired.com
 
(Please DO NOT respond to mcg@netcom.com!)

From bbehlen  Thu Jan 20 16:38:39 1994

>From gdallensworth@ucdavis.edu  Mon Jan 17 13:06:57 1994
Date: Mon, 17 Jan 1994 12:10:11 -0800 (PST)
From: "hazrat Gregor Mosheh (666)" <gdallensworth@ucdavis.edu>
Subject: Bloo Queube
To: My Family in SF <sfraves@techno.Stanford.EDU>

Wow. Ever have one of those nights when the music was perfect, the sound 
system was perfect, the location was great, the hosts and promoters were 
great people, and everyone was so vibed-up that you couldn't have a bad 
time if you tried? That was the party at the Blue Cube.

Resonate, Steve, the same guy who put on the fabulous New Year's Party, 
let Ethan and Bryce use his house for the party. He opened up the whole 
house, bought food, and even provided a terminal (heheheh) for people 
who'd want to log in in the middle of the best party I have ever seen.

All night long, the music was fantastic. I don't know who all played, but 
there were great, every one of them. By name, I know that Seth, Laura, 
John D., and Ethan spun downstairs, while Bryce, Fred (Phred), and Crunch 
played upstairs. Amit came on sometime during the night (who cares about 
TIME with that much vibe going on?) and synthed some really sweet ambient 
tunes. Everybody just poured their hearts and their energy into the party 
and made it a beautiful experience.

One of the highlights of my night, I think, was standing on the steps 
with a bunch of people I didn't know and suddenly saying "Excuse me, but 
I need to go hug someone." Suddenly, some guy who was walking past me 
stopped, turned around, and hugged me! Wow! That was great!

I really don't think I have _ever_ had that much fun at a party. It 
really must suck to be anyone in the world who couldn't make it. Thank 
you, everyone who was there, everyone who helped with it, for making it 
what it was. I love you all!

Our guest speakers are experts on the rave scene and are here today
to comment on Satruday's party at the Blue Cube:
huhuh huh huhuh  That was cool. huhuh huhuhuh He said "vibe." huhuhuhuh
heheh hmm Yeah, that kicked ass. heheheh hmm hehehe hmmm hmm

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 21 11:18:04 1994
From: mcg@netcom.com (MykL G)
Date: Fri, 21 Jan 1994 11:23:01 -0800
Subject: The Saturday alternative
Status: 

I just wanted to remind folx that there's an alternative for those of
us who prefer to dance to a beat that's more funk-influenced and less
"industrial" -- I'm talking about Le Plaisir de Vivre this Saturday.

It's too bad this event had to coincide with Industrial Strength, but
L P de V also spotlights DJ's who are rarely if ever heard in SF --
namely Freddy, Orlando, Bain, and Lil'John from the "Chicago House
Underground".  There's nothing low-energy about Chicago Deep House,
but the pace isn't quite as manic and the sounds tend to be less
harsh on the ears, and the beat is WAY fun-kay.  We're NOT talking
about screaming diva, here!  This is music that makes you feel GOOD!

And if the Chicago posse doesn't do it for you, there's always Garth,
Jeno, Markie, et. al., and an ambient room featuring the unbeatable
Jonah.

Anyway, at least AstroBoy and yours truly will be in that particular
house on Saturday -- let us know if you're planning to be there!

--=-=-===-==---=--=-=-==-=-=-=-=-  MykL --=-=----==-=-=----==--==----=--=-

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 21 13:22:49 1994
From: marvin.tiggerson@aldus.com (Marvin Tiggerson,Cnslt)
Date: Fri, 21 Jan 1994 13:17:58 PDT
Subject: Community Relations
Status: 

Hi folks,

My name is Glenn (aka Tigger) and a group of friends and I have started a
weekly event called the Choc-Lit Factory in Seattle.  Opening night was
last Friday which went off beatifully.  Admission was $5,  w/ free water
and cheap consessions.  The vibe and atmosphere was very similar to what
I've read about your postings regarding Friends and Family.  Lot's o
smiling, happy vibed out folks making connections.  I would especially like
to thank DubTribe for being there and launching a vibe intensity of
inter-galactic proportions.  

The Choc-Lit factory is cooperative venture.  Whatever small profit we've
made so far is being invested right back into it.  Our format will be
changing weekly with various nights dedicated to funk, house,
trance,tribal, etc...  and since the location is in a theater we are able
to change the setup and sometimes have actual built sets from the plays
that are going on during regular hours.  We'll also be incorporating dance
and ritual performances with our parties!

In any case, I would like to get feedback and advise from you sfravers
about what to do in the circumstance that I currently find myself in.
Based on the success of our opening night, a couple of promoters from other
production crews have expressed their concern that a Choc-Lit Factory the
night before their massives would hurt their party.  They have requested
that I NOT do a Choc-Lit Factory on a couple of dates in the upcoming
months.  I've expressed to those promoters that we would be willing to do a
theme or format that would be different (i.e. a funk, or ambient dub night)
so as to not have an overlap as far as the crowd is concerned.  But, they
are adamant about NO Choc-Lit Factory at all.  What do you think, folks? 
I'm trying to find a win-win situation so's that everyone can be at least
be comfortable with whatever goes on.  

I look forward to hearing from you.  Kindess, joy, love and happiness to
you all.

Tigger (Glenn)

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 21 13:43:29 1994
From: Bob Ramstad <rramstad@us.oracle.com>
Date: Fri, 21 Jan 94 13:43:12 PST
Subject: Community Relations
Status: 


well, first off, it's easy to bash the promoters over this, but at the
same time, my first raves were in seattle, and i can understand.  the
scene up there is very small, and it's definitely possible that a
really good vibed-out party the night before a massive might well keep
some of the crowd away.

you are never going to get something which you'll both be totally
happy with, because they've already said that the only thing which
would make them happy is no choco-lit factory the night before, and
you've already said that you want to keep having them (basically)
whenever it works with your schedule.

options:

1) give in.  it's a small scene, there are plenty of nights for
everyone and splitting up the crowd is bad.  i don't think this makes
a lot of sense, though, especially as they are basically telling you
that there are entire weekends that you shouldn't throw a party.  this
sounds greedy and unreasonable.  besides, if they are worried about
their investment, they should shift to smaller more inexpensive
parties.  (i'm sure a lot of your success is due to the very
reasonable $5 cover.)

2) passively advertise the other events at your event.  set up a flyer
table and get flyers from promoters.  they might reach new people and
improve their draw.  this is easy and just helps build the scene,
imho.  you can never lose by advertising other events --- if the other
events are cool, it strengthens the scene, if they turn out to be
lame, you'll get more return customers coming to your event.

3) actively advertise the other events at your event.  poster, large
flyer, perhaps even print the event info for the next night on
"tickets" for your event.  could be considered overly commercial tho.

4) merge.  throw events in conjunction with another promoter.  sell
joint tickets to your event and their event the next night.  have a
special "choco-lit factory MASSIVE" where you provide the style /
direction and the other promoter provides logistics / advertising /
financial backing.  of course there are all the caveats that one
should observe when working with others and $$ is involved.

just my $1.67.  and good job!  i'll have to check it out next time i
get home (probably memorial day)...

Bob

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 21 16:19:31 1994
From: laura@soda.berkeley.edu (Laura La Gassa)
Date: Fri, 21 Jan 1994 16:18:24 -0800
Subject: The Rift Valley
Status: 

Geoff White said something about it being a shame that Industrial 
Strength and L P de V are the same night because it further widens 
the "rift" between the house and techno scenes.

When will people realize that there doesn't HAVE to be a rift.  I've 
been to so many events out East where there were both house and techno 
DJs on the same bill.  It all lies in picking the right DJs and scheduling 
them at the right times.  Heck, F&F had a mixture and that worked really
well.

It all lies in finding a balance.  Some people hate breakbeat, but putting 
a good breakbeat dj on the night will please the breakbeat lovers, not be 
so long as to annoy the breakbeat haters, and might reach the open-minded 
or new people in between.  Ditto for any other sub-genre of dance music.

What I hear isn't so much that people can't stand house or techno or hardcore 
or breakbeat, they just don't enjoy the same stuff all night long.  So mix 
it up and give it variety!  Even I, hard techno lover that I am, get 
annoyed of hour after hour of 160 bpm Belgian mayhem.  I have to be in a 
certain kind of mood and have a certain kind of energy (and a certain kind 
of, er, assistance) to do the all night all hardcore thing.  Last time I 
was in the mood was last April.  It was one of the more brilliant raves I've
ever been to, mainly because I was totally in the mood and I totally wanted 
to be there.

One more thing:  one night of a hardcore/techno rave isn't going to make 
or break any "rift" any more than having one more all-night all-house party.  
It just *is*, that's all.  And besides, if there were only ONE party in all 
of SF worth going to on a given Saturday night, that party would be so big 
and so crowded that it would have lots of annoying logistial problems.
People should have choices, people should have fun.  It looks like there
are a few good choices this weekend, so people will have ample chances
to have fun.

Laura

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 21 16:48:40 1994
From: winston@lurnix.COM (Mark Guthrie)
Date: Fri, 21 Jan 94 14:22:26 PST
Subject: Re: Community Relations
Status: 

Well, about the Choc-lit Factory delema, around here the truly hardcore aren't
stopped from goin out because theyr'e "too tired from the night before" (said
in a whiny voice) and the people who can't go out two nights in a row are just
gonna have to make the decision for themselves as to which event of the two
that they prefer.  Don't let these dudes/chicks dick you.  Promoter cooperation
is great but this sounds like pressure or demands bein put on you.  There are
regular and unregular on every night of the week around here and when/if the
scene can't support it a natural balance happens and the weaker parties don't
make it.  If your parties are good in a large or growing scene there should
be enough bussiness for everybody, if your parties aren't good in a large or
growing scene nobody will come anyway, if your parties are good in a limited
scene they will be chosen over other parties by the people, if your parties
aren't good in a limited scene nobody will come anyway.  Let the people have
choices!

-Mark

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 21 17:14:48 1994
From: csc@pilot.njin.net (Sean Casey)
Date: Fri, 21 Jan 94 20:14:11 EST
Subject: The Rift Valley
Status: 

Yeah it's an awfully big town. Unless the promoters are actively
fighting each other, I don't think competition has to be a rift. 

I think it's really fun to go check out music I don't normally listen
to. Yeah give me a 135bpm slammin' house beat any day. Or some spaced
out hard trance. But recently I've had a good time listening to music
at Spacelab (acid-acid-acid), a cool Chicago-house groove by Mark
Farina in Sacramento, some totally tribal on a DRC tape, and so on.

Long before I ever listened to house, I noticed a pattern in that the
music I really love and stay with over the years I almost inevitably
don't like at first. Don't know why. But knowing that, I give music a
_lot_ of chances before I give up on it.

I'm going to Industrial Strength because I want to hear some *fast* in
your face mind blowing supertweak fucking hardcore. Let's do it!

Sean

About all of this negativity surrounf=ding Sharon.  OK so some of you 
think this is associated with Sharon (I've been told it wasn't), but 
anyway supposing it is.  My first rave almost two years ago was A Rave 
Called Sharon Hardcore 1 and it was one of the best raves I've ever been 
to.  It introduced me in a large way to the rave scene and looking back 
it may have been one of the contributors to the commercialization and 
sudden explosion of the rave scene in the summer of '92 (luckily I was in 
Paris raving!!!)  but when I came back the scene was dilapidated and 
overgrown.  But in reality look what it has turned into.  The real people 
who care are overcoming this to promote smaller parties and a bigger 
sense of community.  Anyway I'm babbling but all of you who are for some 
reason not liking Sharon you need to learn to live and let live and maybe 
even forgive or forget about it and do your thing without stomping all 
over other people's prerogative.

From sfraves-owner  Sat Jan 22 18:15:11 1994
From: child@netcom.com (Spaced-out funky Earthchild)
Date: Sat, 22 Jan 1994 18:15:45 -0800
Subject: Deep house vs. Techno
Status: 


Here's my .01 trillion cents on the Deep house vs. Techno choice.

Certain vibrations of people attract people to different styles, speeds of 
music.  Whatever speed/style of music resonates best with your internal
resonance at a particular point in time, go with it.  

Hard core techno is definitely hard core.  But there are some who can use it as
a vehicle to release pent-up feelings.  And in that way, it can be perfect to 
go with the hard-core flow until it's time to change.

Deep house is definitely deep.  But there are some who can use it as a vehicle
to express or get in touch with some aspects of their inner self.  And in that
way, it can be perfect to go with the deep house flow until it's time to 
change.

Vice versa is true too.  

Why do people have musical tastes??  I ain't gonna ansa dat one, but all I can
say is go wid your flow until you change.  Don't put your preference in musical
taste above others' musical tastes  AND don't allow others to mistake your 
raving about your favorite music as an attack on their fav music.

Who the fuck am I?  I dunno.  I'm just one of the 6.5 billion humans on dis
Earth.  But I love you all and have a good night out EVERYBODY!

Spaced-out Funky Earthchild
[ex-ex-fractal surfer]

footnote: a discussion with raver Morgan helped me come up with this note.

I just returned from an incredible trip to Seattle, checking out the
second edition of Choclit Factory at the Velvet Elvis in Seattle.
This is a now-weekly party being sponsored by a collective group
with major coordination by Tigger (Glenn Fajardo) and Dred-I (Greg
Richards).

I'm cross-posting this to sfraves because I think it's important to
hear how things are going outside the Bay Area.  They're going GREAT!

According to a handbill at the theatre:

  The Velvet Elvis Arts Lounge Theater is dedicated to all that is
  tacky and tragic, classic and kitsch.  Here, a group of actors,
  directors, musicians, designers and visual artists come together
  to work in a "creative incubator" style atmosphere -- a forum for
  local artists to mingle and exchange ideas that will ultimately
  give birth to performance supporting all disciplines.

The Velvet Elvis also sponsors the non-profit YouthCare group which
'has been providing non-coercive programs and services to runaway
and abandoned youth since 1974.  We have developed an outreach program
working with clients from YouthCare through a theater/educational
experience.  The program is designed to give young people practical
technical skills, greater self-esteem and a permanent venue for
exploring their creative abilities.'

Currently, there are two plays going back-to-back at the Velvet Elvis.
At 8 pm they're running 'Kerouac', a 'jazz play' with Vincent Balestri,
and then at 11 pm they have a short drama, 'Snake in the Vein.'

Then at 12:15 the curtain goes down and the Choclit Factory krewe rushes
in to set up turntables and sound, put stage props away, and set up
decorations.  Meanwhile, in the 'lobby' at the back of the theatre,
XLR8R's editor Andrew Smith and Matt Corwine, also both nw-ravers, are
setting up their gear for a chill space effect.

The theatre itself is a typical Pioneer Square Seattle space with brick
walls, high ceilings, a large stage (I know, I swept every square inch
of it three times this morning after it was all over!) and old movie
theater-type seats for the audience.  The stage is also the dancefloor
(a little bouncy, and I could only pine away for the magnificent original
hardwood floor underneath, which very well could be the best dance floor
in Seattle but we'll never know :).  The curtains are pulled back and
the DJs play at the back of the stage for the assembled massive.

And we are talking massive here -- somewhere in the neighborhood of
500 attendees over the course of the evening, and the door crew were
turning people away by 2:30 am (though that lasted only a while).
It's a bit cramped, but very congenial space, what with the tacky
Elvis painting and other miscellanea on the walls.

I liked DJs Masa (who started the evening) and Quest (who finished off
with the final chewn right at 7 am); in between was a long hip-hop oriented
period that I found a bit under my preferred pace, but it was fine to sit
in the chill area with Andrew and Matt providing ambience. 

With an after-party in November after Basics, the New Year's FreeVibe
and one Choclit under their belt, the VE crew did *no* publicity the
previous week and the place was filled but not uncomfortably so.
This shows again how a well-run event built up from months of careful
work can help shift the direction of the scene in an entire area, as
this is clearly doing in Seattle.  

The door cost to support all this -- including rental of the space which
supports the theater and its own artistic and community work, as well as
weekly expenses, DJ stipends, etc.?  

$5.

And it's being done mostly by net.ravers, applying the principles we've
learned and discussed literally worldwide over the last couple of years
here.  This is only one type of presentation that can be made, along 
with anything from house parties to outdoor renegades to true massives.
So I send this out with the advice that we 'keep on keeping on'!

I know it's hard to do this one, much less week after week.  But I'm
impressed by the dedication and spirit being put into this effort.  
When you get to do something you really want to do, there's only one
possible response, "Go for it!"  Come what may, this adds more proof
to what we all know, that the music/dance/culture scene of the future
has more resilience and flexibility than we may at times think.

phred

'Why make it simple and easy
 When you can make it complex and wonderful!'

From sfraves-owner  Mon Jan 24 10:57:41 1994
From: Jon Drukman <jdrukman@us.oracle.com>
Date: Mon, 24 Jan 94 10:55:40 PST
Subject: le plaisir de vivre
Status: 

i had no interest at all in checking out industrial strength, and to
be honest, i wasn't really too psyched for this one either - i feared
it would be crowded and the music would be too housy for me.  but i
went anyway.

first pleasant surprise - i snagged a most excellent parking space.
second pleasant surprise - no line to get in.  third pleasant surprise
- it wasn't even that crowded.

we get in and the back room is where the featured chicago house DJs
are playing - what i heard back there wasn't as bad as i expected, and
in fact some of it was really fantastic - there is a sub-genre of
house wherein they take the typical beat but put really tripped out
technoish stuff on top of it.  i liked that but there wasn't enough of
it, and the DJs typically would switch back to the more commercial
stuff pretty quickly anyway.  bummer.  pluses in this room:
comfortable temperature, plenty of room for dancing.

i ventured into the front room to hear Garth ripping it up with some
hard funky stuff, which caused my feet to instantly launch into Full
On Dance Attack Mode.  unfortunately i had to quit pretty soon
afterwards because the air in this room was practically unbreathable -
it was hot and stuffy and i felt like i was taking in a lungful of
lint with each breath.  so, i went to see what was happening in the
bassment...

downstairs, thomas was playing some stuff with a funky beat but
depressingly mainstream melodies.  i decided to complete my tour of
the club by checking out the upstairs level.

the usual acid jazz crew was in the back room up and the fireplace
room was the chill-out lounge, as always.  i made a mental note to
check back there later.

i went back downstairs to say hello to thomas but he was busy handing
over the controls to jonah of spacetime continuum.  jonah started out
with some nice acidic techno.  i wound up hanging out down there quite
a bit because the highly electronic groove was just what i needed at
the moment.  however, after 4 or 5 records i began to get really
annoyed because jonah has to be one of the worst DJs i've ever heard!
his technical ability is completely non-existant.  i was fighting a
powerful urge to gut him like a trout right there and take over.  it's
no wonder san franciscans generally aren't into techno - so far all
the DJ's i've heard have blown huge chunks!  laura la gassa has more
DJ talent in her pinky than 10 jonahs but she also plays stuff that is
too hard & fast for wimpy old me.  i liked jonah's records (rarely
venturing over 130 BPM), but not in the order he played them!  it was
frustrating, to say the least.  i wish there was a techno DJ in the
city with the talent of a Jeno or Garth... say what you want about
their musical choices - they know how to construct a set and rock a
crowd.

so, back upstairs to the acid jazz room where (i think) julius was
doing a great 100 BPM hip hop set including a wonderful Beastie Boys
track...  unfortunately, he quickly veered off into that soulful
jazz-not-jazz stuff that i hate.  i seemed to be developing a knack
for wandering into a room to hear 5 minutes of good stuff followed by
lots of bad stuff.

so, back downstairs to the house room for a bit.  but 10 minutes in
there and i couldn't take it.

so, back to the front room for someone who i've never seen who was OK
but not too special, and more of that unbreathable air.

so, back to the basement for jonah again who had gone into total
ambience for a bit before cranking in a 135 BPM slammer and then into
some real low energy stuff.  i wanted to clock him so bad...

and then my energy level plummeted through the floor and i just about
passed out on my feet so i decided it was time to leave.

to sum it up: a wildly uneven and inconsistent party with lots of
different types of music to sample and spaces to inhabit.  i really
appreciated the mix of styles, but i found myself wishing that there
was a place i could go to just get into a groove and space out.

Jon Drukman                                         jdrukman%dlsun87@oracle.com
-------------------------------------------------------------------------------
This calls for a very special blend of psychology and extreme violence.

From sfraves-owner  Mon Jan 24 14:47:41 1994
From: mcg@netcom.com (MykL G)
Date: Mon, 24 Jan 1994 14:52:32 -0800
Subject: Re: house & believers
Status: 

Via the nimble fingers of analog resonate:

>         i find that its very difficult to go places that *don't* play house.
>         its much harder to find trance/acid whatever you call it 
>         *synth based dance music* as opposed to *sample* based dance music.

This subject's getting really old, but there are some quick points
begging to be made:

1.  "house" and "other" (acid/trance/analog/techno or whatever) are very
subjective terms, and the boundaries between them are extremely fuzzy.
To me, a majority of what Jeno and at least half of what Garth plays
I would not consider "house" in the strict sense of the word.  There's
acidy and tribal sounds galore at the average SF rave.

>         rap and disco are both the sources of house music and we can get
>         plenty of that.

2.  So?  House, Kraftwerk and Tangerine Dream are the sources of acid/
trance.  Does that allow one to dismiss acid/trance as being derivative,
old, stale?  Although a small percentage of what you call "house" uses
elements of rap and disco (or dancehall reggae, or salsa, etc. etc.), for
the most part house has a feel and rhythm quite unique from its antecedents.
That's one of the great things about house -- it's the universal musical
recipient!  It absorbs 47 times its own weight in other styles, and it
still comes out sounding like new!

3.  There are plenty of DJ's serving up the music you're talking about.
Support events spun by Jonah, Shobhan, Goa Gill, and Ghost, not to mention
Laura.  Maybe Mescalina should start playing out more.  Become a DJ
yourself.  It's a big city -- the more variety the better!

I have never understood people defining their taste in music strictly 
in terms of style.  For any sub-genre you can name of this modern
underground dance music we're all interested in (and there are truly
dozens), there are plenty of records that are lame and a few that are
really well made.  As long as the DJ can build sets with variety and
control the energy of the dance floor, any style of music can be fun to
dance to (for a while).

You already have the solution well in hand -- keep throwing parties like
the one you did last weekend!  I have little doubt that hard trance will
grow in popularity as the year goes on, but try not to diss us house
fans too heavily!

--=-=-===-==---=--=-=-==-=-=-=-=-  MykL --=-=----==-=-=----==--==----=--=-

From sfraves-owner  Mon Jan 24 16:15:02 1994
From: crunch@netcom.com (John Draper)
Date: Mon, 24 Jan 1994 16:15:31 -0800
Subject: Rave report - Kinda long
Status: 

Weekend Rave Report
===================

Greetings Raver dudes all over the world...   Here is yet another report
from the eyes of a dedicated San Francisco 50 yr old raver.

Thursday... Went to SpaceLab which wasn't that well attended this week,
but our very own Ethan (nrg@netcom.com) spun and as expected,  his
awsome record collection and mixing style was second to none.

I wanted to take it a little easy on my injured knee because I want it
to heal before this Saturday,  as I was to go to my very first 100% Hardcore
rave.    I spent a lot of time,  talking to the manager of the Trocadero
about some crazy ideas I had,    then when I got back to the party,  someone
was spinning some rather interesting trancey spacey music that just didn't
pursuade me to do anymore dancing.    Things degenerated rather early
(about 3 am),   So I headed home.

This Saturday,  Spent a lot of time doing my yoga and other exercises,
preparing for "Industrial Strength",  as I wanted to be in top physical
shape to deal with the 160-190 BPM breakbeat and hardcore dancing.

Prior to the rave,  our own Laura,  one of the "Sister DJ's" who often
spins at "Your Sisters house" invites us over for Pizza prior to the
big rave.    There were 3 raves scheduled for this evening...

Saturday, January 22nd - Industrial Strength
        Ron D Core (LA), Deadly Budda (Pittsburgh), Heather Heart (Brooklyn),
        R.A.W. (LA), Demigod (SF), Nikadeemas (Portland).
        hardcore techno, hard trance, breakbeat, house, old skool acid, 
        All ages, all night.  415.979.8411

Saturday, January 22nd - Le plaisir de vivre
        Chicago's Freddy, Orlando, Bain, Lil' John
        SF's Garth, Jeno, Markie, Thomas, Leon
        Ambient & acid jazz w/Jonah, Brennen, Julius
        1015 Folsom, SF.  415.995.4602

Also, REVOLUTION...  not listed in the rave calander.

The new Virgin warehouse that was supposed to host the Industrial
Strength rave didn't pan out,  thanx to the faschist SF police
(which will result in a lawsuit),  so the info line said that
we were supposed to stop by the 16th and Harrison venue (Hays
Theator).  Boy! were we confused...  Was this supposed to be
a Map point?  or the actual location for Industrial Strength?

We were ALL confused,  and didn't know what to expect.   We did
obtain some free tickets to REVOLUTION,  which was ALSO supposed
to be at 16th and Harrison,  so we all (or at least most of us),
decided to head down there,  after picking up some free tickets
to REVOLUTION.   At least it was free.

When we get down there,   they had two lines.   One for Industrial
Strength (which costed $20 to get in),   and REVOLUTION.    Revolution
allowed ins-and outs,  but Industrial Strength didn't.    Yukk!
how TWISTED...   

I think that I should say a little thing about the Hays Theator.
This venue is known for it's faschist goons that are big tough
gorillas that some even carry guns I was told.   They always
have NO ins and outs,  and keep the doors closed shut and it gets
sickly hot and stuffy MOST of the time.    Thay also have a small
outside inclosed area in the back,  and on ocassion,  they allow
people to go outside to chill out.   The Hays Theator has two
main dancing areas,   and for most raves,  both are opened.

In the case where both REVOLUTION and Industrial Strength occupied
the space,  they had the areas split apart,  thus was two seperate
and self contained raves,  and nobody was allowed to go back and forth.

Industrial Strength (IS) was supposed to have this super 80,000 watt
Schredder sound system that was supposed to bring armegedddan to your
brains and ears,   but next door,  it was almost impossible to hear it
unless you went upstairs.  Had they decided to not have the schredder
system?

The  upstairs chill area,  was reserved for the REVOLUTION rave,  but
not the IS.   From upstairs it was possibole to hear the super scredder
sound system through the lazer projection booth,  so I stayed up there
listening for the first time the super fast hardcore sound,  longing
to get down there to try my hand at dancing to it.   My freind Sean
had agreed to help me pay to get into IS,  but I failed to find him.
I guess he went to "Le plaisir de vivre".  Yukk!! and double Yukk!!
as I was drooling and longing to get down there.

Knowing there was a pathway to the other section in the upstairs area,
I checked it out.

Naturally,  a GOON was stationed there,   and I just stood there looking
out the window,  looking confused.  The goon said "Can I help you?",  I
said,  "I'm looking for my friend up here",  and he said "Where did you
come from?"  "Did you come from down there...?",  then asked how I got
upstairs.   I said "I came in the other way".   The goon then pointed to
the downstairs area Where Industrial Strength was happening,  and
asked "Did you pay the $20 to get in down there?"   In my
quick thinking,  I said "Yes!",  and he said,  "OK,  you
can go through here, and opened the door.    I said "Let me
first recover my jacket and umbrella,  I'll be back".    So thats
how I got in.    And BOY!!  It was INTENSE Energy,  but there weren't
many people dancing.    Most folks were all huddled away towards the
front of the room,  keeping as far as possible from the gnarley
loudness of the schredder system.  The noise was tremendiously
loud,  and I normally don't wear ear plugs,   but this time,  I wished
a had some.    There was a place on the dance floor where the sound
apparently cancelled that was ALMOST tolerable.   But I liked the
fact that there was plenty of room to dance,  and boy!! I went totally
crazy out there.   My knee felt GREAT,  and due to the much faster 
beats,  I stayed on the ground,  because the earths gravity was too
low to allow me to get up in the air and down again before the next beat.
But I modified my dance style to keep up with the 190 BPM insansely fast
stuff.  I worked a whole new set of muscles at THIS rave,  and surprisingly
my knee didn't complain at all.

I stayed at IS all night long,  digging the shit out of this (for me)
new and exciting super fast dancing.   Most of the other california
weenies were just standing along the side wondering if they should try
and get out there.  This was the first time I heard "Demigod",  a local
SF DJ.    WOW!!  Sure wish I know how to reach him,  and wonder where
he DJ's around here.    he was totally INSANE....

About 5 AM,   I went next door to REVOLUTION,   after talking a goon
into allowing me to return.   I chilled out a while up stairs for
about a half hour,  did some yoga,  and gave my weak knee a rest,
then danced for a while at Revolution.    Revolution ended promply
at 6 am,  so headed back to IS.   They were still playing that insane
hardcore,  and after my rest,  I really cooked till about 7:30.

Yow!!  I practically OWNED the dancefloor,   as it was almost empty.
Still,  lots of people sere seeking refuge from the super loud scredder
system in the front room,  or next to the wall.   I skittered around
looking totally insane for a total hour and a half of practically
continious hard-core digging it totally...

As good things always come to an end,   a goon finally waddled up to the
stage and said "CUT IT NOW!!",  but the DJ protested,  but finally gave
in,  and faded down the totally awsome cut that was playing.   Good God,
those Hays St goon won't even let the cut end....  What cold bastards
they are....  Then the goon loudly said... Come on.. Clear out NOW!!
I barely had time to recover my umbrella and coat,   and out we went.

Someone handed me a flyer for GROOVE MORNING,  AND I still had my $10
so decided to head down to the ATLANTIS club on 1st and Harrison to
check it out.   It costs $5 to get in,  and I decided it was really
a nice place to hang out on a rainy morning.  They had a very great
chill area,  similar to Boogie Buffet,  and great DJ's and dancing.

I recommend that SFRavers check the place out,  as they go until 3 pm,
and of more people show interest,  they may go on longer.    Sure
beats the EndUp.    

I stayed till about 10:30 AM,  then headed over to the EndUp,  to
see who was there.    Yukk!!  dance floor really wet,   but they put up
some awnings I hadn't noticed before.    Usual stuffy crowd,  a few
ravers from the Old School,   but what the heck... It was free, 
provided you can find a dry spot on the dance floor.    Also,
they had managed to blow out another speaker next to my favorite
dacing spot   Yukk!!  Oh well....  I only  stayed for about 20 minutes
schmoozing w/ a few friends.  Then headed over to the DNA lounge for 
their usual after party.   I still had $5 left,   so didn't balk about 
the $3 cover charge.   Things  were really hopping over there,  but 
not that crowded.    The DNA has a much better sound system then Atlantis,
but nothing compared to 1015 Folsom St venue.

After DNA closed at 2 pm,  I headed back to Atlantis,  and hung out
there till about 4 pm.    Met the people sponsoring Groove Morning,
and noticed the Raves calander didn't have it listed.  Learned
it is now every Sunday... and I highly recommend it.   Good Venue
good vibes,  and it gets my seal of approval.  Check it out..

'Nuff for now...Keep the scene alive...

Cap'n Crunch

From sfraves-owner  Mon Jan 24 17:04:25 1994
From: Laura La Gassa <laura@soda.berkeley.edu>
Date: Mon, 24 Jan 1994 17:04:01 -0800
Subject: Re: INDUSTRIAL STRENGTH !
Status: 

Ethan asks:

 > -If you're not Sharon-related, why was the Sharon HardCore logo on your flier?

Susie explained this one to me:

See, the guy behind the IS party is named Sean (I think?  It was some
male S name).  ANYWAY, the "Sharon HardCore" logo is actually HIS logo,
not Sharon's -- this S-guy worked with Sharon when they started doing
their hardcore raves and Sharon put the S-guys logo on their fliers.
Over time, the S-guy's logo became (mis-)associated completely with Sharon
rather than being associated with someone who worked with Sharon from
time to time.

Laura

On Mon, 17 Jan 1994, Niels P. Mayer wrote:

> 
> Figure out which of these bogus statements was made:
> 	* raving (in detroit) is not about drugs.
> 	* most people at raves drink smart drinks
> 	* rave is just like the hippie scene, except that the focus isn't on

I can safely say that raving in Detroit is not about drugs (born there 
and just got back from a week's visit). There just aren't any reliable 
sources there. And when you do find anything, it's either heavily cut or 
rediculously expensive (or both). Unfortunately, the Motah Town is fueled 
by alcohol, bad pot and attitude. That's why I came out here

#2. <see above> nope, most people drink alcohol.

#3. nope. It's just like the club scene that preceded it. Lotsa attitude 
and in-crowding. Yeah, the hippies had it too, but they generally weren't 
into violence.

Sorry if this topic is way dead. I just got back and I'm now catching up 
on my *267* messages. ugh. JOHN. brrr...

>From jng@soda.berkeley.edu  Mon Jan 24 13:58:51 1994
From: "Joanna [Jo]" <jng@soda.berkeley.edu>
Subject: why pay $20 when ya could've paid $7 ;-)
To: sfraves@techno.Stanford.EDU (SFR list )
Date: Mon, 24 Jan 1994 13:58:24 -0800 (PST)
Cc: vrave@gnu.ai.mit.edu

BASS BASS and more BASS!!!

i decided that saturday's events were much too amusing to leave untold, so
here it is...

after a bunch of people congregated at brian & laura's apt. in 
twin peaks for a pizza get together (which was a lot of fun... thanx so much
to both of you), anne & i were trying to decide whether or not to give up
on the rest of the night or still try to go out.. after hearing that the 
industrial strength ppl lost their original permits for this "virgin 
warehouse" as they put it, and the location was moved somewhere else, _and_
the ticket price was $20, and worst of all, Heather Heart who i'd heard so
much about hadn't been able to make it out here, 
we figured there was no reason for us to go, it simply wasn't worth it..

At the last minute steve (resonate) said that someone has a bunch of passes
to Revolution at 16th & Harrison that night, and so a spur of the moment 
decision landed us (cindy, susie, anne, steve & i) at the venue around 11:05, 
so we were stuck with paying
$7 after all, but we went it in anyway. 

Revolution had some *great* visuals, friendly crowd for the most part, and 
what i thought was the best part of it was the chill-out areas upstairs (which
in the strange lighting looked like rooms in a funhouse..) with plenty of 
very comfy couches & bean bags.. AND they had a coat & bag checkroom (yay,
finally!) 

As for the music.. it was funky, just a bit slow for me...

However, the evening took a much more interesting turn when we discovered,
to my very pleasant surprise, that Industrial Strength was going on 
just next door...

I'd been very much looking forward to this event, dubbed the first legitimate 
hardcore rave in SF.

Susie grabbed me from the other event, and we went upstairs to the laser room 
where the guy in charge of the laser show was sitting, bored out of his
mind and staring in wonderment at "kids our age who had so much energy" 8-)

Many thanks to Paul btw, who not only let us watch the event from
upstairs, but after i wrote out the email addresses for
SFR - sfraves@techno.stanford.edu - & the telnet address for vrave, he
wrote both things in laser and flashed it at the Industrial Strength crowd
for a while ....along with the words.. "for a good time call susie" ;-)

After that, a bunch of us played with the lasers for a while, and at one
point, i was at the helm for about 30 minutes or more, beatmatching lasers to
180 BPM *grin* I think i've found my new calling... ;-)

After that, Susie, with her beguiling charm (;-) susie.. actually, as 
coincidence would have it, she knew the promoter of I.S.) 
managed to get us into the actual event, where we, 
along with anne, stayed for much of the rest of the evening. 

A few comments about the crowd: young highschool-age types, hardly anyone 
was even dancing (tho it's not too surprising since people out here tend not
to know how to dance to hardcore really), rather rude ppl on the whole, i'm 
sad to say, and no chill-out area at
all, extremely crowded..essentially everything that was good about the party
next door was not present here...all except the music..
In short, the event was *definitely* not worth the $20...and to think had
we gotten there 5 minutes earlier it would have been free 8-)

Susie kept saying "oh these songs are so old" but it was stuff that i've never
gotten a chance to hear at any event, so it was all new 
to me.. and while Easterners may scoff at me for enjoying Deadly Buddha's
set, i thought it the best one of the night, except for DRC (IMHO, still the
one and only DJ goddess 8-)

So this bass bin worshiping girl had an incredibly wonderful time... i am
very glad i remembered ear plugs tho, since i seemed to spend most of the
time right in front of the speakers, completely drenched in sweat but 
with a huge grin on my face...
i danced so hard that i can barely move today, but it was worth it... 
we practically mopped the floor with our hair we were headbangin' so much ;-)

Long Live Hawdcoah! ;-) what can i say...i love it.. i couldn't get enough of 
the pulsing, breakneck speeds and pounding rhythm...i just wish there was a lot
more of it in this city.

my idea of the perfect rave.. the crowd and scene at Revolution with hardcore
and hard trance... well, i can dream, can't i? 8-)

And we have at least one new convert to the realm of hardcore madness.. 
Anne (Boobo) recently indoctrinated into the SFR scene ;-)

Many thanks to susie once again, without whom it have been a much less 
interesting night out, no doubt...8-)

So now i'm just waiting for the next one, with any luck, maybe it won't be
too far off - it would be wonderful to get Heather Heart
to make it out here sometime soon. 


-jo aka SF bass bin worshiping vampyre ;-)


"BRING ON THE HARD DRIVING BASS RHYTHM!"

Jeno spun downstairs, and it was just as rockin' as the front room was before
they closed it. 

I really liked this party and I really liked the chill space 
that was upstairs beyond the acid jazz room. Nicely lit (with a fireplace!) and
quiet enough to reasonable hold a conversation. Now, that's what a chill room
should be. I'm tired of chill rooms with DJs, because the chill room DJs 
invariably play their "ambient" music up so loud that you can't hear anything
else. If they alway had this room open I would enjoy going to 1015 Folsom a
whole lot more.

Pete

Subject: weekend report
Status: 


We checked out the wicked late nighter on friday night in the bassment
of  1015 folsom. a very good party, with very good music and very nice
people, IMHO. After raving from 2:30-7AM friday night, I felt
absolutely no need to go out on saturday.... THe party was a bit slow
to start, but it just got better and better and better, peaking
around 5-6AM.

THe only unfortunate thing is that I didn't see any of the familiar faces
I usually see at raves. Don't quite understand why.

$7.00 well spent.

From sfraves-owner  Tue Jan 25 02:18:50 1994
From: turpin@apple.com (johnnymartian)
Date: Tue, 25 Jan 1994 02:17:32 -0800
Subject: Digital Media Alliance Diagram
Status: 


If anyone is interested in a very colorful, creative, and somewhat
accurate, connection diagram, showing the alliances, investments, and
ownerships of most all companies in the digital domination race, check out
pages 58 & 59 in the January issue of "New Media" magazine.

It includes Baby Bell Companies, Cable Companies, Telecom Companies, Movie
Studios, Major Broadcasting Networks, Settop Box companies, Online
Services, Game Companies, Interactive Title Companies, Software Companies,
Publishing Companies, Music Companies, Consumer Electronics Companies,
Computer Companies, and of course the Internet.

There seem to be some pretty major connections missing though, maybe they
have left it as an excersice for the reader to finish.

Sorry to burst your bubble BUT,

Here's my theory on this drug violence poll:  NBC is cashing in on 900 numbers.
Regardless of what you or others say, NBC will say x% supports, y% is against,
where x and y are made up.  Ever notice how the percentages reported on TV or
in newspapers are such neat and even percentages like 60% or 80% instead of
49.8% or 27.3%?  Ever notice how the sample size is almost never mentioned?
Ever notice how it's not even mentioned how they obtained their sample?  I've
taken 2 courses in advanced statistics and I'm always wondering how they came
up with THEIR "margin of error".

As for drug violence, I think that it is a fostered part of American society.
It's fostered by the media and by the police.  It's all one large game.  
Violence is used as a bargaining chip by the government to increase their 
surveilance of every-day Americans.  In my humble opinion, most Americans 
believe what they see on their nightly "world" News or the local news or major
newspapers like the NY Times, Washington Post, etc, and it is very sad.  I
recommend that you see "Manufacturing Consent" a documentary by Noam Chomsky
playing at the Red Vic in upper Haight on Feb 19 & 20th.  It will be crowded
so get in line early if you go.

It may be time to start venturing out to check data that you hear via the media
to see if it's really true.  You may be surprised by what you may find.  Don't
be lazy and believe the news, check it out for yourself!!

If NBC was really interested in the opinions of it's viewers, it would have
listed a non-toll number or an 800 number for YES or NO.  Don't get sucked
into this "express your views, it's important to us at your cost" game.
If 20,000 people call at 55c each, that's an easy $11,000.  But I must say
that even if these were not toll numbers, it still wouldn't make much of a 
difference on what THEY choose to or DON'T choose to report.

Bugeye

From sfraves-owner  Tue Jan 25 08:04:52 1994
From: ccat@netcom.com (Chris Beaumont)
Date: Tue, 25 Jan 1994 08:05:27 -0800
Subject: Codes for Kids
Status: 

Sunday's NYTMagazine had an amusing bit in its "Sunday" column, titled:
 
HTIJX KTW PNIX
 
"The scrambling and decoding of electronic communication is one of the
hottest issues in cyberpolitics.  The Government would like to have
the key to all communications.  Export of cryptographic software is
restricted.  Both software companies and independent groups like the
Cypherpunks argue for the rights of individual privacy."
 
"All of which makes Microsoft's new Creative Writer software for
children intriguing.  It includes some primitive cryptographic tools
to enable kids to encode what they write ... to hide it from curious
siblings and F.B.I. agents."
 
What surprised me was the next line -- "Theoretically, this product
would come under export restrictions, even though the code would not
challenge a spy, let alone a bright 9-year-old."
 
After explaining what a Caesar substitution cipher is, the bit goes
on to note that "Presumably, Microsoft will not face any
difficulties in exporting its product even though it comes from
the K.G.B. (Kids Games Business) at Microsoft.  On the other hand,
it does instruct the youth of America in the basics of electronic
privacy."
 
In case you're wondering, the encrypted phrase is "Codes for Kids."
 
Lee Tien

                        ROBOT SUMO COMPETITION
Date: Fri, 21 Jan 1994 21:20:18 GMT
Lines: 59

The San Francisco Robotics Society announces a Robot Sumo Competition to be 
held on Sunday, May 1, 1994 at the College of Engineering at San Jose State 
University in San Jose, California. 

Robot Sumo is a contest in which two robots attempts to push one another from 
a small (approximately 5 feet in diameter) circular pad. The robots may compete 
in one of two classes, radio-controlled or autonomous. They are restricted in 
size (must fit in a 20 cm x 20 cm footprint) and weight (3 kilograms).

The defining rules of the contest are set by Fuji Software in Japan. A copy of 
these rules is available from the San Francisco Robotics Society Bulletin Board 
( 415-648-6427) or they will be included in return confirmations to registrants 
for the contest.

There is no fee to register for the contest. You may enter as manyn   ___

From sfraves-owner  Tue Jan 25 15:50:33 1994
From: LUKEY@WordPerfect.com
Date: Tue, 25 Jan 1994 16:11:34 -0700
Subject:  house & believers -Reply
Status: 

>>>>>>>>>>>>>>>
	rap and disco are both the sources of house music and we can get
	plenty of that.
<<<<<<<<<<<<<<<

I don't think so...  There is some house music that has been
influenced by disco (disco house), but house music has been around
for much longer than you or I, and certainly much longer than the
disco movement.  House music came from jazz.  Listen to all of the
basic underlying chord progressions, piano tracks, bass tracks, drum
tracks, etc.  It's very easy for me to see the origins of the house
music that I like, but I guess it depends on what type of house music
you like.  House music has probably been around for a lot longer than
jazz really, if you consider the meaning of the word house.  House
music has always just been music made by everyday people, in their
own house, or for friends visiting their house, or for somebody
else's house.  It has never really meant to be like a sell-out
100,000 attendance live house concert in a football stadium.  Just a
few thoughts.  Don't take any of this personally.

From sfraves-owner  Tue Jan 25 18:29:04 1994
From: graham@cyborganic.com (Graham Potter)
Date: Tue, 25 Jan 1994 18:27:08 -40962758 (PST)
Subject: A simple spiritual high
Status: 


Every day on our way home from work, Gillian and I have looked at the
scaffolding on the mural at the Women's Building, eagerly awaiting its
dismantlement. Today as we walked by, workers were taking down the
last metal bars and wooden planks in the moist evening air. The work
of art they so casually unveiled rivals anything I've ever seen for
its spiritual intensity, political savvy, brilliant color, and awesome
size. It depicts on one enormous wall the all-encompassing energy of
the human existance from the woman's perspective. Make a point of
walking by that spot sometime in the next few days, while the paint is
still fresh. It's on 18th Street between Valencia and Guerrero.

woman) and my friend anne up to watch *and the laser guy let us play

by this stairway so i went to go and take a look.  I convince the security
that we really belonged at the IS party and they let us through.  So we
hung around that party for a while and the tunes were pretty good and the
crowd was so so. Also the visuals other than the laser at IS sucked
compared to the video screens and lights they had next door at the
revolution party.  So after a while of sneaking back and forth i met up
with sean ralston who i had met when i spoke down at the board of
supervisors.  He told me that a california state court overturned the
curfew law but now the police are enforcing another section which says
that they can by there discression decide whether or not to grant 1
night party permitts.   So he and Larry Murry are sueing a
whole bunch of people including mayor jordan and the
police (cool!!)  Sean then introduced me to deadly buda from pittsburgh,
who knew all the net ravers from there (ie diesel boy, mike b, joe lesene)

on at 6 am and heard part of her killer set (which resonated the
entire room) and went home to get some sl**p.  ahhhh

From sfraves-owner  Wed Jan 26 13:09:49 1994
From: JMRTNKO DSGN ETC SFCA <martinko@netcom.com>
Date: Wed, 26 Jan 1994 12:34:31 -0800 (PST)
Subject: my sf anniversary. thanks.
Status: 


Well, two years ago today at just about this time, I made San Francisco 
my home. I remember upon getting off the plane thinking I had 
accidentally landed in Bedrock. The flora around SFO looked very 
neanderthalish. One thing was certain. It was *warm*! This little fact I 
take for granted now (esp. after just getting back from a week home in 
Detroit) was a *big* deal to me then. Most of us wouldn't think of 
wearing shorts yet, but I had 'em on in minutes after landing. Needless 
to say, the bus ride coming up 101 through the hills for my first 
real-life look at my new city was amazing.

Not knowing a soul, a luckly roll o' the dice landed me in the SOMA 
warehouse I still live in now. The folks who became my family have now 
all moved on elsewhere, but new friendly faces have appeared here and 
there to fill the gaps. 

My first real SF rave experience came a month after moving here. I hung 
out at the Top for a while and overheard people talking about the Full 
Moon later that night. After asking around a bit, a few great guys 
offered me a ride there (later turning out to be Martin and Jeno). Other 
than Berkeley, I hadn't been out of the City so I was worried about just 
how I was going to get back home, but it was a little too late to think 
about it as we passed over the bridge into Vallejo. I was going to go 
even if I had to walk back home. Later standing on a lonely hilltop 
looking down on several hundred people dancing to the best music I've 
ever heard (in or out of Detroit), the lights of Vallejo, and the sun 
just starting to come up, I knew I had found the place where I most 
wanted to be.

I don't know many of you, but you all sound just as friendly as the great 
people I've met so far. I don't know of any other city where someone 
knowing noone with nowhere to live and no job can just arrive with a 
suitcase and begin to make a future for themselves. Thank you all. And I 
want to extend a warm welcome to those brave and hopeful people that, 
probably as I type, are getting off their plane right now, with only 
their suitcase and a smile. Good Luck.

I hope to see some of you at SpaceLab this Thurs. I'm gonna be celebrating.

JMAR. best damn two years of my life so far. sounds like there's gonna be 
a third!

From sfraves-owner  Wed Jan 26 13:56:26 1994
From: marvin.tiggerson@aldus.com (Marvin Tiggerson,Cnslt)
Date: Wed, 26 Jan 1994 13:47:43 PDT
Subject: RE: More Choclit
Status: 

Hi Henry!!!

Thank you for your insight and encouragement. And yes, your "rambling"
makes a lot of sense.  I agree with you that I need to do whatever I need
to do to send out the message of the vibe and cultivate this "scene" into a
viable community.
For the most part, I think that the other promoters have been supportive of
what we've been doing with the Choc-Lit Factory.  However, regardless of
how important the message is, I need to find a way to communicate that
message in a way that comes across effectively and in a way that would
benefit everyone around.  In other words, instead of saying "Fuck you Mr.
Promoter, I'm gonna have this party no matter what!!", I feel that a more
effective way would be to say, "I understand your concern but, what we're
doing is not a matter of competing with your event, it is a cultural
endeavor.  What can we do to work this out?".  The Choc-Lit Factory was
started with the same principles as the FreeVibes in the sense that the
party will be paying for itself to keep it alive and going as long as
possible while minimizing the impact that the financial factors will have
on the party and it's partycipants. The Choc-Lit Family does need to be
sensitive to the needs of other members of this community.  If we find that
what we're doing is going to be detrimental to our community we need to
take a step back and look at what we're doing and why.  For me it's a
matter of constantly taking my bearings and doing reality checks as we
travel along a an ever changing landscape.   Again, thank you for your
insight and support.  Please keep the feedback coming!!!

Kindness, Joy, Love & Happiness to you

Tigger(Glenn) 


_______________________________________________________________________________
From: Henry Kumagai on Wed, Jan 26, 1994 11:59 AM
Subject: More Choclit
To: Marvin Tiggerson,Cnslt


Regarding your message about when to throw parties:

You have a responsibility to throw your party when you want to, 
regardless of pressure from other promoters.  You have to provide 
direction in this community as a promoter and organizer.  What is a good
rave
for you?  What does positive spirit mean to you?  If you think you 
may know and have the means to provide people with a taste of it, do it.  
Why do you have to compromise your good intentions and positive 
commitment to appease another organizer?  They may not know what you know 
or have the same good intentions.  Maybe they do.  The point is that if 
you feel that you are doing the community a service, advancing the cause 
of this house/underground/party/together/rave thing, you need to bring it 
to everyone so they can join in and support your commitment.
Does this rambling make sense to you?  I hope so.

It's all about empowerment.
-Henry

Also, look at it this way. If it was a 'regular' phone number, the call
would probably call you 55 cents anyway... but you would pay it to the
phone company instead of NBC. 900 numbers that charge very small amounts
like 55 cents are really just a way of redirecting your money from AT&T
to whoever is running the poll/service/etc.

Of course, it would be better if the poll was an 800 number, but you should
realize 800 calls aren't really free, they are just paid for by the callee.
Then NBC would be paying out lots of THEIR money just to run a poll, which
isn't incredibly fair, and not very businesslike.

From sfraves-owner  Wed Jan 26 18:32:23 1994
From: pkennedy@xsoft.xerox.com (Paul F. Kennedy)
Date: 	Wed, 26 Jan 1994 18:32:11 PST
Subject: more on the drug issue
Status: 


This article was in Monday's Guardian (London) International newspaper ...

		Ecstasy a soft drug says Spanish Court
			by Jane Walker in Madrid

Spanish anti-drug officials are alarmed at a weekend ruling by a high-court judge
in Madrid which defines ecstasy as a soft and non-addictive drug which does not
damage health.
  The ruling came at the trial of three men accused of trafficing the popular 
"designer drug", and means that they now face only five-year sentences and fines 
of 50 million pesetas (about #250,000).  Prosecutors had sought the 11-year 
sentences and higher fines prescribed for dealing in hard drugs.  Spain's penal 
code differentiates between soft drugs - such as marijuana or hashish - and hard 
drugs, which include heroin and cocaine, amphetamines and, until now, ecstasy.
  The four defence lawyers assembled a team of expert witnesses, several from the
United States, who stated that ecstasy, a cocktail of chemicals known as MDMA, was
different from a similar drug, MDA, commonly known as "the love pill" which has 
dangerous side effects.
  They said MDMA was not hallucinogenic but "accentuates visual, tactile and aural
senses and produces a sense of well-being and greater empathy.  "It is not known
to cause physical or mental dependancy, and it's effects only last for two to 
three hours."  Their controversial evidence contradicts other medical experts who
believe that long-term use of ecstasy has psychological side-effects and causes 
dependence.
  Ecstasy first appeared in Spain in the 1970's, particularly on the island of
Ibiza, where it became popular in tourust discotheques.  The drug became more
freely available and is widely taken by young people embarking on the "bakalao
route", a binge beginning in Madrid and other big cities on a Friday night and
continuing through the weekend to throbbing bakalao music.  It often takes the
form of a pub crawl which involves driving at breakneck speed along main roads 
and ending in bars on the Mediterranean coast.  Many of the weekend road accidents
in Spain involve young people.  Drugs, both hard and soft, are openly sold in 
and around bakalao bars but most of the young people who take part in the raves
say they prefer ecstasy.
  It now appears that what they have been taking could have been MDA.  "It is 
important to become aware that what is sold as ecstasy on the bakalao routes
is often not that drug and could be more dangerous," said Jaime Sanz de
Bremond, the main defence lawyer.  The prosecutor in the case intends to appeal
to Spain's supreme court.  He is certain to be supported by the interior
ministry's anti-drug campaign.


--

Pk.

From sfraves-owner  Wed Jan 26 21:05:09 1994
From: "Niels P. Mayer" <mayer@netcom.com>
Date: Wed, 26 Jan 94 21:04:39 -0800
Subject: Re: NBC poll and other ings 
Status: 


Ummm... maybe someone mentioned this before (i'm 143 msgs behind on
sfraves), but with a 1-900 or 1-800 number, the people on the other end
can easily get your phone #, and from that determine the caller, and
from that they can probably correlate your vote to the number you are
calling from, and perhaps to the caller him/her-self. This is
especially true for a 1-900 number where they have to know who to bill.
SO I WOULDN'T CALL THIS NUMBER UNLESS I WAS AT A PAY PHONE, unless one
is willing to express views that correlate to legal practices.

I don't know much about phone systems, but I've seen and heard about
such "caller id" systems with mine own eyes and ears. Back when I was
employed at HP Labs, the HP Response Center was using some of my UI
software (WINTERP) for interfacing to such "caller id" 1-800 machines,
amongs other things, so I got to see what they were doing first-hand.

When a customer called for software support, the "support engineer"
could do an early determination of who they were because the caller id
phone system would output the number from whence the call originated.
This would speed the process of ensuring that the "complainer" had
software support; for example, if the originating number had been used
previously, a form would pop up containing the people most likely to be
at that number, any "software support identification" numbers, previous
complaints or request for support, etc.

It's pretty easy to extrapolate from such a system, which are in wide
use among large US businesses, to a system which logs the phone numbers
of people which have views that aren't in synch with the law.

Just call me paranoid, ok?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=
==           Niels Mayer -- netcom!mayer -- mayer@netcom.com               ==
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=

From sfraves-owner  Wed Jan 26 22:27:05 1994

Subject: Manufacturing Consent
Status: 

Via the keyboard of bugeye:

> I recommend that you see "Manufacturing Consent" a documentary by Noam
> Chomsky playing at the Red Vic in upper Haight on Feb 19 & 20th.  It will
> be crowded so get in line early if you go.

Actually, this is a documentary *about* Noam Chomsky, filmed by Mark Achbar
and Peter Wintonick, co-produced by National Film Board of Canada.  I can't
recommend this highly enough -- I saw it twice during its run at the Castro
last year.  Considering the seriousness of the subject matter, the production
is actually quite snappy and entertaining.

See it -- you'll never view the media in quite the same way afterwards.
You'll understand exactly how futile it is to try to do things like "use"
the media to our advantage.

number. They combine this with the content of other big databases and
decide that since you are 'pro drug', used your ATM card to buy distilled
water and miso at Safeway for 3 weeks in a row, and are paying a monthly
payment on a motorcycle that mailing you ads selling vacation packages
isn't likely to result in a buy, but that a plea for money to save the
rainforests stands a chance. Then they sell this data to other big
companies.

Not to mention they get a big news story 'from the people' and you pay for
the research.

my $.02.

Date: Thu, 27 Jan 1994 13:29:26 -0800
Subject: Re: NBC poll and other ings
Status: 

Jamie L sez:

> I think the data (which I am sure is being collected) is more likely for
> marketing. They get an opinion to map you to a demographic and a phone
> number. They combine this with the content of other big databases and
> decide that since you are 'pro drug', used your ATM card to buy distilled
> water and miso at Safeway for 3 weeks in a row, and are paying a monthly
> payment on a motorcycle that mailing you ads selling vacation packages
> isn't likely to result in a buy, but that a plea for money to save the
> rainforests stands a chance. Then they sell this data to other big
> companies.
> 
> Not to mention they get a big news story 'from the people' and you pay for
> the research.

I think this is all very true, but there's another angle to these 1-900
polls.  I believe the media is using them to *calibrate* how well they are
manipulating public opinion, and using the results to tweak the techniques by
which they present the news in order to more effectively control people's
opinions.  On the drug legalization issue, maybe the results showed them
they have to change tactics.

In case you think I'm a hopelessly paranoid conspiracy maniac, here's a
story that was buried comfortably inside this Tuesday's Chronicle (and before
you scoff, it was reprinted verbatim from the Washington Post):

--------------------

MEDIA HAVE STOKED ANXIETY ON CRIME
By Richard Morin, Washington Post

Washington, Monday January 24,

In the past few months, crime has soared to the top of the country's short
list of major problems, replacing the economy in most surveys as the single
most important problem facing the country.

Yet some serious students of public opinion view this increase with a
disquieting sense of deja vu.  They wonder whether current concerns about
crime are being driven by the media rather than by crime statistics.

Such doubters recall a similar spike in public concern about drugs during
the 1980's.  Scholars now convincingly argue that the concern about drugs
was largely prompted by the media's sudden interest in the subject, rather
than by an increase in the prevalence or use of illicit narcotics.  They
note that when the media lost interest in the drug story, it fell like a rock
from the top of the polls.

Similarly, we may just be embarking on a similar roller-coaster ride over
crime.

First, a bit of statistical history.  In January 1989, 19 percent of those
interviewed in Washington Post-ABC News polls named drugs as the "most
important problem" facing the country.  In October, more than half of those
interviewed -- 53 percent -- said drugs were the country's biggest problem.
The pattern was as apparent in other major polls.

But just as quickly, the bottom fell out.  By September 1990, only 16 percent
named drugs as the nation's most important problem.  This year, drugs elicit
no more than a single-digit response in the polls.

As it turned out, both the boom and the bust of public concern about drugs
were out of step with reality.  Data from a number of large-sample surveys
taken during those years suggested that drug use during this period was,
if anything, flat or perhaps falling slightly -- anything but up.

What had increased and then dropped out of sight in this period was the
media's attention to drugs as a social issue.

"The New York Times discovered drugs, and thereafter many news organizations
discovered it," said Maxwell E. McCombs, a professor of communication at
the University of Texas.  "The reality was that nothing had changed regarding
the incidence or use of drugs."

Current concern about crime may be following a similar pattern.

The percentage naming crime as the nation's most important problem rocketed
from 5 percent in June to 21 percent in the November Post-ABC News poll.  A
similar trend has been captured in other surveys.

Meanwhile, nationally and in most states, the overall crime rate has been
essentially flat or rising at an incremental rate.

Part of the focus on crime could be attributed to the apparent health of
the nation's economy.  "It could well be," McCombs said, "as the economy
moves off the agenda, then in effect it opens the door to another issue to
move on the stage, and crime may be it."

--------------------

I'd add to that last paragraph, that by keeping the masses of TV-fixated
Americans obsessed with issues like crime and pro sports, they don't have
time or energy left over to pay attention to really important issues such
as truly effective health care reform, the real causes of the homelessness
problem, etc.  And why don't they want people to pay attention to these things?

Remember that the networks are dependent on the advertising income of
insurance companies, real estate conglomerates, medical equipment and
drug manufacturers who DON'T WANT people to demand a change to status quo
in areas where it would affect their bottom line.  And don't forget that
NBC is actually owned by one of the largest military contractors and
manufacturer of megabux medical equipment, GE.

They'd much rather have the public screaming at politicians for a "three-
strikes-you're-out law" (which, by the way, most prosecutors, prison
officials and academic criminologists say will actually make the problem
worse, and is much less cost effective than putting money into drug treatment
and rehabilitation programs), than have them screaming for real medical or
real-estate reform.

And it's *so* easy for them to create this climate by bombarding us with
wall-to-wall coverage of the Polly Klaas case, Nancy Kerrigan, etc.  Can
you believe how blown out of proportion this story about Kerrigan and Harding
has been played, in comparison to its actual importance?   It turns out that
the  very same day Kerrigan got bopped on the knee resulting in a minor bruise,
a pro basketball player named Larry Stewart got his home broken into, was shot
in the neck (one inch from his spinal cord) and stabbed in the thigh.  That
story ran as a few paragraphs deep in the sports section, while Kerrigan
was plastered all over the front pages for weeks.  You don't suppose it
might be because Stewart is a black male and Kerrigan a white female by any
chance???   Nahhh....

Regardless of whether you agree with this reasoning, you'll find Manufacturing
Consent to be quite eye-opening...

And now... back to our regularly-scheduled hardcore vs. deep house flame war...

If you've ever heard of the movie Plan 9 from Outer Space, there is a
sequel coming out soon called Plan 10 from Outer Space, that isn't
made by the original producers of Plan 9.  The final scene was filmed
at Playscool, a local (to Salt Lake City, anyways) performance arts
center/dance club that plays house music.  The last scene is a rave
with aliens and mormons (members of the LDS church).  If you're not
familiar with what mormons are, just picture stuffy religious types
in suits and dresses, and aliens from outer space having a rave
together.  It should be really wild when it comes out.  The original
plan 9 wasn't that hot, but the guys that produced this one know how
to do it right.

Subject: accessing techno.stanford.edu archives (fwd)
Status: 

Let me answer his questio{ before he gets a chance to ask it :)

To FTP from techno.stanford.edu, type:

ftp techno.stanford.edu

for the login: put "anonymous"
for the password: give your email address.

And then you can go from there, using "dir", "cd", and "get" (also type
bin if you're transferring binary files).


	Brian

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 28 00:43:36 1994
From: phred@well.sf.ca.us (Fred Heutte)
Date: Fri, 28 Jan 1994 00:43:15 -0800
Subject: Re: why i don't go to fmr
Status: 

Awright you dweebs!!!  CHill!!!!   :)

None of the handful of FMR's I've been to have been truly outstanding.
But it doesn't matter, it's always an adventure.  If only I knew it
would end up being tonight I would have flown down for the weekend a
day early.  As it is I'll prolly see y'all from the plane as we pass
over the Marina :).  

Like all events these are not for everyone, Wicked have their own
style and interests musically, but let's give credit where due for
their persistent efforts which have, after all, really changed the
scope of the r*** scene in SF overall, in my opinion, for the better.

But I don't hold it against anyone if they don't like/attend FMRs, no indeedy.
There's nothing wrong with having likes and dislikes as long as we let each
other hold our own counsel and make this whole thing grow through the
diversity of our preferences and opinions.

As an archeologist, I learned long ago that typologies are a trap.
Drugs vs. non-drugs.  House vs. hardcore.  Vinyl vs. CDs.  Analogue vs.
digital.  Indoors vs. outdoors.  Floppy hats vs. baseball caps (OK,
I'm a partisan on that one :)

What *does* matter is attitude and open-ness and struggling to break
the bonds we encircle ourselves with.
