From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 28 01:46:08 1994
From: mcg@netcom.com (MykL G)
Date: Fri, 28 Jan 1994 01:51:16 -0800
Subject: Re: why i don't go to fmr
Status: 

Via the neuroanatomy of analog resonate:

>         sci-fi trance music is the thing to play

I happen to be listening to a *really* excellent bit of that right now.

It's the "Pure, Impure" EP by Seefeel.  Tracks 4 and 5 are coproduced
by Aphex Twin.  Highly recommended for those who haven't given up on the
processed-guitar-loop-trance approach of My Bloody Valentine, etc.

The reason I don't go to FMR's is because they never start 'til 3 or 4,
so unless you go to bed *real* early the night before and head down to
where it's happening an hour or two before sunrise (and if you're *extra*
lucky, they haven't been busted into submission that particular night)
it's just not worth hanging around cold parking lots for hours on end.

But that's my own humble non-declarative opinion, and for those of your
who are into the thrill of the chase for old time's sake, and other
die-hards, I do wish you a most transcendent of night/mornings.

--=-=-===-==---=--=-=-==-=-=-=-=-  MykL --=-=----==-=-=----==--==----=--=-

>	i'd like to see live acid-jazz bands, and definitely live

>	been to. the problem was it was misfocused. if there was no
>	*stage* with lights directed towards it and was organized in
>	more of a warehouse style party it would have been perfect.

orbital are a terrible example of live techno, if you ask me.  yes,
they DO supposedly actually manipulate the sounds but their setup
doesn't seem to have a whole lot of interactivity.

now Dubtribe on the other hand are probably my peak example of live
techno.  they don't have any "songs" per se, just "groove elements"
that they can mix and match and arrange live to respond to the crowd's
energy.  and oddly enough, i don't think they sound all that great on
record because the crowd dynamics are missing!

>	how many of you out there have electronic music setups that you
>	use to generate real-time dance music? how many of you would be
>	willing to schlep your gear and set up and play?

jeff and i tried doing this but it's a lot of effort and i really hate
the thought of lugging our gear all over the place - a lot of it is
very fragile.

/jon

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 28 12:00:19 1994
From: mcg@netcom.com (Michael Gold)
Date: Fri, 28 Jan 1994 12:00:52 -0800 (PST)
Subject: Re: why i DO go to fmr
Status: 

OK, so I'm a mere human which entails a certain inevitable degree of
self-contradiction...  life would be so BORING if everyone were 100%
consistent, don't you think?  =)

The FMR this morning was inCREDible!!!  I was just about to blow it
off as usual, but I was struck by a burning hunch at about 5:45 am
that this one would be really special and I *had* to be there...
so off I went...

The place was a dump... literally... a spiralling jetty of probably toxic
landfill jutting off the shores of Albany, just behind the race track.
But this site is the ULTIMATE FMR site -- loads of parking, but a twenty
minute hike over the landfill before you could even hear a HINT of the
sound system.   Almost unimaginably remote -- the dance area was a
sort of (un)natural amphitheatre molded out of twisted hunks of metal
and gnarled entrails of some earlier reinforced concrete structures:
perhaps the remains of the Cypress Freeway after the '89 quake?  Not
even the remotest chance of any authority figures turning up -- the
effort it would have required just wouldn't have made any sense.

Gotta give the FMM crew credit -- they did a LOT of prep work getting the
site together, marking the safest trail down to the dance area (there
were definitely some majorly hazardous obstacles to encounter along
the way.)  People were treating the site as an ongoing art project, painting
the twisted fragments of metal with day-glo acrylic colors.  One had
the feeling of dancing INSIDE a huge installation of post-modern sculpture.

I touched down there at my usual preferred strategic time, about 45
minutes before sunrise, dawn just starting to break as the moon was setting
so picturesquely DIRECTLY over Mt. Tam.  Yes, just like the Berkeley Marina
you could get a fish-eye view of the whole Bay Area, all those bridges and
islands, the downtown skyline looking like a toy model, and seeing Highway 80
coming to life but oh so far away...  But this time no early morning joggers
and dog-walkers giving us odd looks...  Just the right number of people
(maybe 200 or so).

And then, the music -- Garth and Jeno were pulling out tracks from
somewhere between Leftfield and Neptune...  deep acid, trance, house,
and every flavor inbetween.  And it all seemed to fit together underneith
the clear, crisp morning skies...  Actually, Garth was playing a Bjork
remix at the moment I arrived, which caused the corners of my lips to
connect with my ears in a grin the likes of which my face hadn't experienced
in untold months...

Greetings and hugs to all net.friends who were there:  the Wings, Marlo,
Geoff, Ravi, TAXI (oops, I've blown his cover!), and who out there remembers
Shaun Ralston from his brief stint on this list?  And of course Gregg, who
popped over after sunrise to see how his original brainchild worked out
(and seemed rather pleased).  Paul K -- I hope you made it safely!

Anyway, just when one least expected a major comeback from the FMM, they
gave this observer a morning to remember like none other since the Bonnie
Doon classix...  Maybe the fact that this one was one day late, technically
speaking, was probably a major factor in its success in an odd sort of way.

And to the Wicked Posse -- a three-month period of immunity from my usual
jaded cynicism!  =)

----____----____--__--__--__--__-_-_-_-_-_-__--__--__--__--____----____----
    MykL G            Clue In, Groove On, Peace Out      mcg@netcom.com
____----____----__--__--__--__--_-_-_-_-_-_--__--__--__--__----____----____

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 28 12:23:22 1994
From: Bob Ramstad <rramstad@us.oracle.com>
Date: Fri, 28 Jan 94 12:23:13 PST
Subject: night report
Status: 


went to spacelab, got there early (10:45ish).  crowd is very different
every week.  this was a pretty cool crowd, but small.  as people left,
more vibe-ridden people took their place.  max was around ten people
on the dance floor, but everyone was going well.  Shobhan didn't
really get it on, but Alex did.

many people were asking for the FMR info from me.

i left spacelab at 2 with every intention of going home, but decided
to spend the extra $1 on bridge tolls and drive over to the site.
parked my car and within 2 minutes Trish came up and asked me for a
donation :-)  i consider this to be the ultimate sign that the party
will actually happen, it means that a reasonable subgroup of the
wicked posse are already on the site.

hiked in about a mile, got lost in the landfill, started wandering
around, found the party.  took until 3:30 before the music came on.
nice party, site was cool overall, and definitely non-bustable, would
have required a LOT of police effort to bust it.

intermittant generator problems kept the music going on and off
through 5 am, when i left.  seemed like the party would go off nicely
if they managed to get the generator going consistently.

glad to see that they still happened, and i'm not sure, but imho, it
was a good site and will be filed away in my data banks for future
parties... lots of parking and pretty hard to bust, far away from
residences.

Bob

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 28 13:39:23 1994
From: pkennedy@xsoft.xerox.com (Paul F. Kennedy)
Date: 	Fri, 28 Jan 1994 13:39:01 PST
Subject: last night's Full Moon Rave
Status: 

MykL,

Thanks for calling me last night.  Yes, I did make it to the FMR.  I understand 
this was a brand new location and it did resemble a post-apocalyptic 
landscape.  It was a little unsettling at first.  Me and my friend stood 
around for what seemed like an age before the music started.  Full marks to Alan 
and the others in Wicked for going to a hell of a lot of trouble to put this on.

It wasn't a wholly successful night. There were a few teething problems, 
as you'd expect with any new space.  It was unfortunate that the generator 
wasn't operating very well.  This meant that from about 4:00am until 4:30am, 
every few minutes it would sputter, cut-out and the music would die, then 
it would respark, the power would flow again and the music would come back.  
Too bad, but nothing Wicked could do about that.  Then there was a longer 
break later on in the night while someone went off and got gasoline for the 
faulty generator ??? I found myself sitting on the raised landscape above 
the dance area and looking at the lights on four sides.  Great view of SF, 
the Golden Gate, Oakland, everywhere in the north bay. Crowd were friendly 
too.  I didn't think walkways were marked at all, I didn't see any marking 
myself. I was afraid, actually, that someone would do some real damage to 
themselves by falling as they tried to descend into the dance area.

It was funny, at one time the generator was coughing and failing to fire and
it would spew out these noxious fumes, for a minute I thought Wicked had
supplied dry ice machines !  I was soon enveloped in what turned out to be 
harsh, acrid carbon-monoxide fumes.

My friend was visiting from LA and she commented that it was just like home 
to her...  lots of pollution, tent cities, evidence of earthquake damage 
everywhere and collapsed freeway sections thrown in as well.
On the nitrous issue, yes it was there again last night, lots of it. 
I have never done it myself but my friend tells me that it isn't a pleasant
experience.  She is very familiar with the LA scene (she's a writer, one 
of her articles is stored on techno.stanford) and said that the
thing that was a bodyblow to the scene there was the death of three guys
who were in a van with a large tank of nitrous that had a leaky valve.  
They asphyxiated from the fumes and it bought a lot of heat from the law.

It would've been a very special night last night if the generator didn't 
persistently deflate the mood by failing.  The music at the start was a 
bit hit and miss as well, it was only after a long time that it settled 
down into consistently good trance.

The fairy lights that were strung of the industro-sculpture were cool, 
winking on and off while the music played.  Also, I was glad of the opportunity
to give Wicked a few bucks to help offset the expenses for this.  I pity the
folks who have to drag all the gear back that long path to the parking lot.

I drove back home to San Mateo at 7:30 and surfaced too late, missed
an important meeting.  But I'd do the same again.  And my friend said she
wished LA had events like this.  It no longer does, so make the most of
them while you have 'em.

Thats what I think anyway... thanks again to Wicked.

Pk.

From: brycer@netcom.com (Bryce Ryan)
Date: Fri, 28 Jan 1994 13:48:10 -0800 (PST)
Subject: RFC--PreParty on 2.19.94?
Status: 

Hi, folks!  Hope you all are doing well.

I was casting about for dates and times, jonesing for a party in February
of the kind that only SFRaves can do.  So, I looked at the calendar, and
considered that February 19th seems to be an ideal date for a party...the
Saturday of a long weekend.  However, close consultation with the calendar
reveals a benefit for Malachy that night.  Hmmm.  I had an idea that I 
wanted to share, and request your comments on.

Would folks be interested in a "pre-party" that day, say from 4PM to 10PM?
Filled with that special SFR magic where we all pitch in what we can?
I really want to support the Malachy benefit, and I'm sure that many of
you do, too.  I also (personally) want to get together with you all in
a more intimate setting to catch up on happenings, gossip, network :), and
do the things we love to do together.

I would be happy to help organize and coordinate everyone's efforts if 
folks are into such a thing.  We would need a space, a sound system
appropriate to the size of that space, DJs, and anything else you want
to bring that's fun.

Please email me with your comments, suggestions, and offerings if you're
interested in such an event, or have feelings about it.  I will summarize
to the list.

Thanks!  I look forward to seeing you soon!
--

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 28 14:27:28 1994
From: Bob Ramstad <rramstad@us.oracle.com>
Date: Fri, 28 Jan 94 14:27:15 PST
Subject: last night's Full Moon Rave
Status: 


well, not to bring up a huge thread that's been beat to death here
previously, but nitrous is the only drug that MIT tells its students
that if you are caught with it, you will be thrown out, no excuses
taken.  many people have died at MIT doing it in areas which aren't
all that sealed.  many people have also died from sealing the area to
intensify the effect.

if you are going to do nitrous, use balloons and do it outside.  the
biggest danger in this case is hitting something hard (like the
ground) when you pass out.  this danger shouldn't be minimized, i have
friends with facial scars from hitting a gravel surface.

if you do it inside, have the windows open and good air circulation.

the negative PR associated with nitrous is incredible precisely
because people die from it all the time.  nitrous has no oxygen in it,
and using a lot of it will make your lips and hands turn blue.

imho, on the other hand, proper use is as safe as a trip to the
dentist :-) tho there are those who would argue with this and claim
that the brain is damaged by the lack of oxygen (i.e. some people
argue that most of the effects are from oxygen deprivation than an
actual effect of the nitrous).

yow!

Subject: ???
Status: 

Oh Goodie!!  I got a little time to whip up a report on the Full Moon
Rave last evening....

Not sure that FMR was even to take place,   I headed into SF early
to try and score some IOB.    After that,  I headed over to Space Lab
and got treated to the most awsome trancey tunes I've heard since listening
to a German mix I got from my German friend.    It was really good
hoppin 145 BPM acid trance.    Yow!!   I couldn't sit down,   it just 
grabbed me by the "Wazoo" and didn't let go.

After a while,   I decided to give my knee a rest (And some ice),  and
hung around and schmoozed for a while.    I met Martinko 
(martinko@netcom.com) for the first time.

Brian was there,  and gave me the FMR location and asked me not to
give out the info till after 1 am.   Besides,  I was having so much fun
trancing out to some incredably awsome trance.

Eventually,  all good things must come to an end,  so after the plug
was pulled on SpaceLab,   I headed east over to E. Bay and somehow
managed to locate the FMR,  which was literally in the dump.   It
was a large landfill area near the race tracks in North Berkeley.

As I approached the parking area,  I saw at least 50 - 60 cars neatly
parked in a very large parking area.    I saw other groups of people
heading in the general direction of the bay,   on this small penninsula
made out of landfill.    The walkway was rather tretcherous,  and
one had to be careful not to get stabbed by concrete re-enforcement
rods.    Knowing how clumbsey I am,  especially in dark area,  I
had to be extra careful.    The IOB didn't help matters either.

I kept walking and walking and STILL didn't hear any music.    Some
people were leaving the area,  and politely told me to "Follow the
arrows",   which really helped out.     I must have walked 
about a half mile,   and faintly in the distance,  I heard what 
might be the music.    Sure enough,  it was getting louder,  so I must
still be going the right direction.    This was about 4 am when I arrived.

I wandered sround,  looking for little arrows,  and eventually,  just
behind this mound,  I could hear people cheering and blissing out.

The "Gas heads" were out in force that evening,   and I don't even want
to know how many nitrus tanks were whooshing. 

First thing I did was to locate a nice spot to taste more of this IOB.
I wanted my head to be in the right space for THIS event.

Finally,  as I headed over to the dance area to get into some pretty
good tunes,   the music stopped.   Yukk!!  generator failed.   I 
commented "Someone forgot to feed the generator!".    The music
eventually started up again,   but didn't last long.    Apparently the
generator was making a last gasp for survival.    Eventually,  things
started up again,   and at least a good hours worth of dancing out in
the clear moonlight really put my luv phazers on to full blast.

Then the music stopped,  so I decided it was good time to chill out
and schmooze for a while.    Really good crowd this morning.   The
music wasn't going to start up again,   until someone volunteered to
walk all the way back for more gas.    At this point,  I decided to
go and get some food.    It was about 6:00 am.    As I headed over to 
the freeway,   I noticed an unusual amount of cars,  and I figured that
if I waited another half hour,   I would never get mack to Marin,  so
I headed home.

This FMR location has to have been the BEST site for a rave then anything
I've seen so far.    A little more effort to clear away the junk,  and
a very large dance area can be realized.

They also had decorated a mound side with multiple colored lights that
added to the visuals.

So,  in closing I would say that this FMR was a really good one.   I'd
say at least 200 plus people attended,   although a tad more descrete
as lots of people didn't know of its location.

Later... Keep the ecene alive!!
Cap'n Crunch

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 28 18:32:54 1994
From: pkennedy@xsoft.xerox.com (Paul F. Kennedy)
Date: 	Fri, 28 Jan 1994 18:32:35 PST
Subject: the nitrous and alcohol issue
Status: 

Jon,
 
>> On the nitrous issue, yes it was there again last night, lots of it. 
>> I have never done it myself but my friend tells me that it isn't a pleasant
>> experience.
 
> nitrous is *great* fun!  however, i wouldn't want to do it at a
> rave... it's the ultimate crawl-inside-yourself drug and raves are
> about opening yourself up, not shutting yourself off.

Lest you or anyone else think I'm tsk tsk-ing the use of nitrous, I would
like to say that I am not.  All I've personally heard about nitrous seems 
to indicate (to me) that the possible positive effects from doing it are 
outweighed by the possible negative consequences.  Just like skydiving.  
And what I've read from other contributors to this list today hasn't
changed my opinion ! I merely mentioned it in the FMR context because 
in the recent past there has been a lot of comment regarding those seen 
as wrong-type-ravers who take nitrous at renegades.  I was simply trying 
to paint an accurate picture of what I saw, no judgement should be implied.

Actually, this prohibition-like attitude puzzles me.  Alcohol is frowned 
upon at these parties, I know.  This may be because of litter problems 
posed by bottles and cans after the event, but I think it's discouraged 
because it is seen as a nasty intoxicant, likely to be a catalyst for 
arguments or violence.  I'm of the opinion that if you are an aggro-prone 
individual, you are probably going to be difficult to encounter regardless 
of whether you've imbibed e, nitrous, alcohol or indeed nothing at all.  
Jeez, I like a good gargle every now and again myself, it doesn't turn a 
timid individual like me into a nutter.  And I suspect that a guy who goes 
nuts when he's had a few drinks isn't going to turn into a lamb if he 
takes e.  There doesn't seem to me to be a direct provable correspondence 
between menacing behavior and any one particular substance that we routinely
see at these events.

The coolness-hierarchy (unless I'm misreading what I've seen on this list)
applied to these substances seems to me to be wrongheaded.  Any of the 
substances I've mentioned here can be lethal in string doses.

As far as I'm concerned, the only rule is "Take whatever you want, just 
respect yourself and others".  And don't do anything stupid like overdose
or asphyxiate. 

Besides which, the music is the important thing.

Pk.

From sfraves-owner  Fri Jan 28 22:24:49 1994
From: rox@netcom.com (Roxanna Stieber)
Date: Fri, 28 Jan 1994 22:25:15 -0800
Subject: Re: why i don't go to fmr
Status: 

>wow I didn't know ravers like guitar music.

I like many different kinds of music, and yes I like Spaceman 3.

There wouldn't be so many problems with nitrous if people would fill the
tank 50/50 with nitrous/oxygen. That's what people used to do in the
70's. Actually they would fill one tank with oxygen and another one with
nitrous, then they would fill a single size water bed with 50/50 and attach
a one way valve with a short hose to it. This would be in the center of the
room with everyone sitting in a circle around it. Then the hose would make
its rounds with the others pushing on the mattress. BTW , to state the 
obvious, the bed was empty to start with. Anyway at that time it was great
fun. I haven't done it since then because straight nitrous gives me a 
horrid headache and I don't like the idea of killing off my brain cells
by aphyxiating them.

Rox

From sfraves-owner  Sat Jan 29 10:26:12 1994
From: howardb@enlil.premenos.sf.ca.us (Howard Berkey)
Date: Sat, 29 Jan 1994 10:20:43 -0800 (PST)

> FIGURE OUT A WAY TO *MAKE* IT HAPPEN, THAT'S PART OF THE FUN!!!

Well, being 700 miles away will make it hard for me to help much :-)

Still, I hate to say it but Phred's right. (not that I hate to say
that Fred's right, it's just a big bummer that it couldn't happen).
There are at least 2 insurmountable problems to a big party there.

1.  Proximity of anal retentive neighborhood
	An SF equivalent would be trying to throw a renegade FMR on
the Presidio golf course.

2.  Parking
	While there are 3 lots nearby, they are either patrolled (like the
one by OMSI) or in view of a major road (the one by the rose gardens).
Other than that it's all narrow roads.


Also, it's been a couple years since I went to a party up there, so
this phenomenon may no longer exist, but for some unknown reason,
parties in the park, esp. near the rose gardens courts, *always* seem
to have  huge numbers of people hanging around in anticipation, no matter how
"private" the party is supposed to be.  Parties have been busted an
hour before they were supposed to start because police came up and
dispersed the crowd waiting for the party! 

(Portland police are notorious for this kind of thing, indeed it might
be their primary function.  We used to joke that if the police were
around you were guaranteed to be safe, since they were guaranteed
never to be anywhere near an actual crime.... they're too busy raiding
the Satyricon or other clubs. :)

I have no idea why it happens, but it would be hard to keep people
from visibly milling around the area before the party.  It's like
local high-school partiers can sense when a party is going to go off.

-H-

While reading alt.rave today I came across Captain Crunch's 
review of the FMR.  

Was it really a good idea to post the location of the FMR site -- even 
after the fact -- the a world-wide distributed newsgroup?  Especially
since this was a virgin site? I thought Wicked was feeling that the FMRs 
were getting too big and that they should go deeper underground.  

Just wondering.

I know I'm being paranoid, but I think it's a good idea to keep 
re-usable renegade location information quiet.  

Sometimes a little caution is a good idea.  All we need is a bunch of 
careless people to use a good renegade site for some wild beer party 
(or even a rave) and trash the place or have someone get hurt and then 
*blam* we lose another good site.

Laura

Personally I think that there are not going to be people on sfraves or
alt.raves looking for renegade spots where they can go have wild beer
parties anyway.  I'm in Oregon right now and when I come back for the
summer I think it's great that if someone says "it's at the landfill" I'll

think are going to sit around and write this stuff down.  For instance if
you told me Bonny Doon or the Water Temple or Candlestick it's as simple as
that, I don't need further info I know what is being talked about.  This
way the directions become simpler for the people who care to know and
remember.  Just my own opinion.

From sfraves-owner  Sat Jan 29 15:08:40 1994
From: jsl@netcom.com (John S. Lee)
Date: Sat, 29 Jan 1994 15:09:04 -0800
Subject: FMM and the planet...
Status: 

I dunno wassup wit' dat!

Others have said it but here's my .02:

Some things should be kept amongst family.  Posting the location or a clear
description of the whereabouts has the potential of alerting certain
authorities in such locales.

Hey, the cops KNOW what's going on (the may be cops, but they aren't *that*
stupid).  So why talk about the location...why talk about it to those not
involved with the SF scene (i.e. not on SFR List)?  Hell, it's a free
country and you can say what you please, but don't forget about the
consequences of being so free with info, there is more than the individual
to keep in mind (at times).

Oh well...I wish I understood why things are done.  Maybe then I would
understand why certain things get undone.

Audi 5.

> parties anyway.  I'm in Oregon right now and when I come back for the
> summer I think it's great that if someone says "it's at the landfill" I'll
> know just about where it is.  Most people who don't care too much I don't
> think are going to sit around and write this stuff down.  For instance if
> you told me Bonny Doon or the Water Temple or Candlestick it's as simple as
> that, I don't need further info I know what is being talked about.  This
> way the directions become simpler for the people who care to know and
> remember.  Just my own opinion.


Site will ALWAYS have limited usefulness, what should happen is that there
is a pool of renegade sites, so that we can alternate and not burn any single
site out.  If we used Bonnie Dune for each and every FMR then the authorities
would feel compelled to take a position, (this is what happened to Berkeley
Marina) the best thing to do is to keep looking for fresh spaces ALL THE
TIME and don't use any one space too much.  I think a small (50 -100 person)
party could work at the water temple if it were kept quiet and was executed
properly (no cars to give it away, I have a plan if anyone is interested :)
a FMR would never be allowed to happen however.

From sfraves-owner  Sun Jan 30 10:19:51 1994
From: jsl@netcom.com (John S. Lee)
Date: Sun, 30 Jan 1994 10:20:25 -0800
Subject: New parties....please.
Status: 

The only "bad" thing about some of the parties is that they never seem to
end.  There is always the "last" of something or other.  I think that
things come to an end, and it's best to end a project as gracefully as
possible.  But always telling people that it is the "very last", and then
to put on another party with the same name, just makes people go "er, uh,
but I thought...".

The Gathering, Wicked, A Rave Called Sharon are well over a couple of years
old now and are in need of progeny.  Just a few suggestions:

The Gathering's progeny should be called Gathering II or Perpetual
Gathering, or Gathering Returns.  It's obvious it ain't goin' away any time
soon so (besides, it's GREAT to see a Gathering that does not use the
Jeno/Garth combo! Right On!!!)...

Wicked should spawn the Son Of Wicked or The Boyz Bollocks or just plain
Bollocks.  What was once "the one", has become another club.  Like an old
man, it very well could be past it's prime.  Time to pass it on to a
yungun... (Markie is STILL the man tho' !!!!).

A Rave Called Sharon should just stop.  Besides the fact that it's namesake
is now married, and the party should rightfully become community property.
So it's spawn would be more properly named A Rave Called Mrs. Preston, or A
Rave Called Sharon & Preston, or A Rave Called Community Property. In any
case he (Halim) needs to pay people before throwing another party....I
guess a more apt name would be A Rave Called The Check Is In The Mail.

I dunno about other parties, I am just watching as each one goes on for 365
days.  Once it reaches that age, I think it's time for the soap factory.

Audi 5,
John

From sfraves-owner  Sun Jan 30 16:55:07 1994
From: janie@netcom.com (Janie Fogel)
Date: Sun, 30 Jan 1994 16:55:44 -0800 (PST)
Subject: TV is evil
Status: 

No, tv is not evil.  It's a source of good and bad information and requires
selectiveness just as the net does.  I don't watch an immense amount
of television, but i have seen some great programs dealing with nature
archaeology, news, art....to decide that any medium is worthless cuts
you off from the world.  There are times that i become frustrated with
Usenet i.e., bogus news groups, flames, people with limited views...
however, i would never want to unsubscribe because i know that there is
much to receive and learn.  TV is the same; perhaps the problem is
self-control, just turn it off when you can't find something worthwhile.

From sfraves-owner  Sun Jan 30 17:09:05 1994
From: grigsby@agames.com (JUMP IN THE FIRE)
Date: Sun, 30 Jan 1994 17:07:08 -0800 (PST)
Subject: Why I don't own a TV
Status: 


> 	i own no tv.
> 	that solves the problems.
> 
> 	i suggest this lifestyle to everyone.
> 
> 	its amazing how different the world is when you *never*
> 	watch tv and have its distorted and polluted visions poisoning
> 	your brain.

No shit.  You suddenly have all this _time_ to do things you didn't
before.  I can play guitar, bass, drums, and enough keyboards to make
synth muzik -- I have read and done enough to consider myself an
authority on science fiction, situationism, ecotopianism, anarchy,
co-operatives, and New Hype Religions, and still have time to be
"into" tekno, punk, weird electronic knobtwisting, and the occasional
foray into newspapers just to see how boring people live, not to
mention writing arcade games for money.

Television is an _inherently_ fascist medium, because the tiny group of
people who control the broadcast control the dissemination of information
to millions who watch it.  This information bottleneck at the top
guarantees easy control of content, as anyone who has participated in
an event later covered by the news can attest.

And I don't have stupid commercials running through my head all the time --
at least no more than when I stopped.

If you want to do fun things, get rid of your TV, and suddenly they will
become possible.  I shit you not.

// g

From sfraves-owner  Sun Jan 30 18:04:11 1994
From: robhon@netcom.com (Rob Honeycutt)
Date: Sun, 30 Jan 1994 18:05:44 -0800
Subject: Re: Why I don't own a TV
Status: 


>Television is an _inherently_ fascist medium, because the tiny group of
>people who control the broadcast control the dissemination of information
>to millions who watch it.  This information bottleneck at the top
>guarantees easy control of content, as anyone who has participated in
>an event later covered by the news can attest.
>

I really hate aguing the side of TV, I'm a habitual non-watcher :-), BUT...
I would have totally agreed with this statement 15 years ago, i.e.,
CBS,ABC,NBC.  (Oligarchy rather than Fascism.)  I would argue that this is
becoming less true every day.  Those old ivory towers of communications are
crumbling very rapidly.

I would also argue that there is very little in the way of information
being disseminated.  It's a noise to information ratio of about 1000/1.
The answer is not to just never watch, you just have to have a mighty fine
filter!

Oh!!  There is a GREAT book on this very subject that everyone should read!
It's called "The Age of Missing Information"  by Bill McKibben.  McKibben
collected all the television footage broadcast in one 24hr period via a
cable network in Fairfax, VA and watched all of it (including some 25
episodes of The Brady Bunch).  Then he compared that "information" with the
information he gained by taking a 24hr hike in the Adirondack Mtns.

rob.

From sfraves-owner  Mon Jan 31 06:52:37 1994
From: resonate@netcom.com (analog resonate)
Date: Mon, 31 Jan 1994 06:53:13 -0800
Subject: gear buying + info:
Status: 


	Many of you aspiring electronic musicians are always looking for
	the instrument of the day to make those happening sounds.

	Here are some tips from an owner, restorer, and user of
	vintage mechanical, analog, and digital electronic music
	devices:

	Never buy anything from Guitar Center. Ever.
	Never buy anything from Sam Ash.

	Never accept ludicrous prices for gear that is single purpose:
	i.e. Roland TR-808, TB-303, Moog, and all other non-MIDI
	synths.

	If you want to buy from a dealer who will support it for
 	90 days and give you a reasonable price call up
	Starving Musician in Santa Ana, CA, or Carusos Music in
	New London, CT. I don't have their phone #s off hand.
	If you wan't to buy a Sequential Circuits box then call up
	Wine Country in San Jose.

	If you can, try out the gear and push every single button
	and twist every single knob on it. 

	Do the knobs make noise? Do some of the buttons not work?

	If something isn't 100% functional make sure you haggle a 
	good price for it.

	Always ask for the manual. If they dont' have it
	haggle a better price. Complex gear needs manuals.

	Try to buy efficient musical instruments. A TB-303 is a very
	simple synth and you can spend far less money for far more
	powerful devices that will be more useful i.e. any
	MIDI controlled analog synth. Same with a TR-808.

	Don't expect to MIDI up your old analog gear unless you have
	a lot of money and/or have good electronics skills.

	Beware of samplers that have proprietary storage. If the
	discs aren't 3.5" and the hard drive isnt' SCSI then don't
	buy it.

	And the most important point: dont' try to sound like
	everyone else. We have way to much TB-303 sounding stuff.
	
	if you have any *serious* questions about electronic music
	gear please email me.

	(((((((((((resonate)))))))))))
	resonate@netcom.com

	p.s. Aphex Twin (Richard James) does NOT use homebuilt/home mod 
	     stuff on tour. It was all commercial boxes: Synthi, Korg, 
	     Sequential Circuits, Roland. Ditto for Vapourspace.
	     Aphex Twin runs his whole show from his Sequential 
	     Circuits Studio 440 sampler/sequencer/smpte generator.
	     Thats why he can crouch down and not move for the whole
	     show. Its all pre-programmed. (surprise surprise)

From sfraves-owner  Mon Jan 31 09:30:00 1994
From: mcg@netcom.com (MykL G)
Date: Mon, 31 Jan 1994 09:35:09 -0800
Subject: Privacy As Roadkill
Status: 

Forwarded-From: "Andrew Anker" <anker@spcom.com>
Origanlly-From: Brock N. Meeks on Sun, Jan 30, 1994 9:17 PM
Subject: Privacy As Roadkill
To: com-priv@psi.com
 
Jacking in from a "Private No More" Port:
 
Washington, DC -- If privacy isn't already the first victim of
roadkill along the information superhighway, then it's about to be.
 
A law enforcement panel addressing the Administration's Information
Infrastructure Task Force Working Group on Privacy told a public
meeting here last week that it wanted to "front load" the National
Information Infrastructure with trap door technologies that would
allow them to easy access to digital conversations;  eavesdropping
on any conversation or capturing electronic communications
midstream.
 
But only for "the bad guys."  Us honest, hard working, law abiding
citizens have nothing to fear from these law enforcement agencies
selling out our privacy rights to make their jobs easier.  Nope, we
can rest easy, knowing that child pornographers, drug traffickers
and organized crime families will be sufficiently thwarted by law
enforcement's proposed built-in gadgetry for the national
information infrastructure.
 
There's just a small problem:  Law enforcement agencies, any law
enforcement agency, has yet to prove it needs all these proposed
digital trap doors.  In fact, according to a U.S. Assistant
Attorney appearing on the panel, "Right now most law enforcement
personnel don't have any idea what the NII is."
 
Gore Gives Go Ahead
===================
 
Panel members, representing the Justice Dept., FBI and U.S.
Attorney's office, said that they took Vice President Gore's
promise that the White House would work to ensure that the NII
would "help law enforcement agencies thwart criminals and
terrorists who might use advanced telecommunications to commit
crimes," as tacit approval of their proposals to push for digital
wiretap access and government mandated encryption policies.
 
Gore buried those remarks deep in a speech he made in Los Angeles
earlier this month when the Administration first fleshed out how it
planned to rewrite the rules for communications in a newer, perhaps
more enlightened age.  Those remarks went unnoticed by the
mainstream press.  But readers here were forewarned.
 
Fuck Ross Perot's NAFTA-induced "giant sucking sound."  That
"thump" you just heard was Law Enforcement running over the privacy
rights of the American public on its way to the information
superhighway.  The real crime is that the collision barely dented
the damn fender.
 
This cunning and calculated move by law enforcement to install
interception technologies all along the information superhighway
was blithely referred to as "proactive" law enforcement policy by
Assistant U.S. Attorney, Northern Dist. of California Kent Walker. 
Designing these technologies into future networks, which include
all telephone systems, would ensure that law enforcement
organizations "have the same capabilities that we all enjoy right
now," Walker said.
 
With today's wiretap operations, the Feds must get a court to
approve their request, but only after supplying enough evidence
warrant one.  But Walker seemed to be lobbying for the opposite. 
Giving the Feds the ability to listen in first and give
justification later was "no big difference," he said. Besides, "it
would save time and money."
 
It's Us vs. Them
=================
 
For Walker privacy issues weighed against law enforcement needs are
black and white, or rather "good guys" vs. "bad guys."   For
example, he said the rapid rise of private (read: non-government
controlled) encryption technologies didn't mean law enforcement
would have to work harder.  On the contrary, "it only means we'll
catch less criminals," he said.                    
 
But if law enforcement is merely concerned with the task of "just
putting the bad guys in jail," as James Settle, head of the FBI's
National Computer Crime Squad states, then why are we seeing an
unprecedented move by government intelligence agencies into areas
they have historically shied from?  Because law enforcement
agencies know their window of opportunity for asserting their
influence is right now, right at the time the government is about
to take on a fundamental shift in how it deals privacy issues
within the networks that make up the NII, says David Sobel, general
counsel for Computer Professionals for Social Responsibility
(CPSR), who also spoke as a panel member.
 
"Because of law enforcement's concerns (regarding digital
technologies), we're seeing an unprecedented involvement by federal
security agencies in the domestic law enforcement activities,"
Sobel said.
 
Sobel dropped-kicked this chilling fact from behind the closed
doors of the Clinton Administration into the IITF's lap:  For the
first time in history, the National Security Agency (NSA) "is now
deeply involved in the design of the public telecommunications
network."
 
Go ahead.  Read it again.
 
Sobel backs up his claims with hundreds of pages of previously
classified memos and reports obtained under the Freedom of
Information Act.  The involvement of the NSA in the design of our
telephone networks is, Sobel believes, a violation of federal
statutes.
 
Sobel's also concerned that the public might soon be looking down
the throat of a classified telecommunications standard being
created.  Another move he calls "unprecedented," is that if the
NSA, FBI and other law enforcement organizations have their way,
the design of the national telecommunications network will end up
classified and withheld from the public.
 
Sobel is dead bang on target with his warnings.
 
The telecommunications industry and FBI have set up an ad hoc
working group to see if a technical fix for digital wiretapping can
be found to make the Bureau happy.  That way, legislation doesn't
need to be passed that might mandate such FBI access and stick the
Baby Bells with eating the full cost of reengineering their
networks.
 
This joint group was formed during a March 26, 1992 meeting at
FBI's Quantico, Va., facilities, according previously classified
FBI documents released under Freedom of Information Act. The group
was only formalized late last year, working under the auspices of
the Alliance for Telecommunications Industry Solutions (ATIS).  The
joint industry-FBI group operates under the innocuous sounding name
of the Electronic Communications Service Provider Committee
(ECSPC).
 
The ECSPC meets monthly with intent of seeking a technological
"solution" to the FBI's request for putting a trap door into
digital switches that would allow them easy access to those
conversations. To date, no industry solution has been found for the
digital wiretap problem, according to Kenneth Raymond, a Nynex
telephone company engineer, who is the industry co-chairman of the
group.
 
Oh, there's also a small, but nagging problem:  The FBI hasn't
provided a concrete basis that such solutions are needed, Raymond
said.  CPSR's Sobel raised these same points during the panel
discussion.
 
The telecommunications industry is focused on "trying to evaluate
just what is the nature of the [digital access] problem and how we
can best solve it in some reasonable way that is consistent with
cost and demand," Raymond said.   One solution might be to write
digital wiretap access into future switch specifications, he said.
 
If and when the industry does find that solution, do you think the
FBI will put out a press release to tell us about it? "I doubt it
very much," said FBI agent Barry Smith with the Bureau's
Congressional Affairs office. "It will be done quietly, with no
media fanfare."
 
Is it just me or are these headlights getting REALLY close?
 
The FBI's Settle is also adamant about trap door specifications
being written into any blue prints for the National Information
Infrastructure. But there's a catch.  Settle calls these "security
measures," because they'll give his office a better chance at
"catching bad guys."  He wants all networks "to be required to
install some kind of standard for security."  And who's writing
those standards?  You guessed it:  The NSA with input from the FBI
and other assorted spook agencies.
 
Settle defends these standards saying that the "best we have going
for us is that the criminal element hasn't yet figured out how to
use this stuff [encryption and networks in general].  When they do,
we'll be in trouble. We want to stay ahead of the curve."
 
In the meantime, his division has to hustle.  The FBI currently has
only 25 "net literate" personnel, Settle admitted. "Most of these
were recruited 2 years ago," he said.  Most have computer science
degrees and were systems administrators at time, he said.
 
You think that's funny?  Hell, the Net is a still small community,
relatively speaking.  One of your friends is probably an FBI Net
Snitch, working for Settle.  Don't laugh.
 
Don't Look Now, Your Privacy Is Showing
=======================================
 
The law enforcement establishment doesn't think you really know
what you expect when it comes to privacy.
 
U.S. Attorney Walker says:  "If you ask the public, 'Is privacy
more important than catching criminals?'  They'll tell you, 'No.'"
 
(Write him with your own thoughts, won't you?)
 
Because of views like Walker's, the Electronic Communications
Privacy Act (ECPA) "needs to be broader," said Mike Godwin, legal
services counsel, for Electronic Frontier Foundation, speaking as
a panel member.  The ECPA protects transmitted data, but it also
needs to protect stored data, he said.  "A person's expectation of
privacy doesn't end when they store something on a hard disk."
 
But Walker brushed Godwin aside saying, "It's easy to get caught up
in the rhetoric that privacy is the end all be all."
 
Do you have an expectation of privacy for things you store on your
hard disk, in your own home?  Walker says that idea is up for
debate:  "Part of this working group is to establish what is a
reasonable expectation of privacy."
 
That's right.  Toss everything you know or thought you knew about
privacy out the fucking window, as you cruise down the fast lane of
the information superhighway.  Why?  Because for people like
Walker, those guardians of justice, "There has to be a balance
between privacy needs and law enforcement needs to catch
criminals," he says.
 
Balance, yes.  Total abrogation of my rights?  Fat chance.
 
 
Meeks out...
 


--=-=-===-==---=--=-=-==-=-=-=-=-  MykL --=-=----==-=-=----==--==----=--=-



From sfraves-owner  Mon Jan 31 09:36:04 1994
From: csc@pilot.njin.net (Sean Casey)
Date: Mon, 31 Jan 94 12:35:52 EST
Subject: TV isn't the real evil
Status: 

Television is an electron beam sweeping across phosphors. It's an
output device.

Most Television Programming is not, shall we say, utilitarian. It is
designed specifically to make money by making us pay attention to it
as long as possible. That being the driving force behind its existence
it is not surprising at all that things have reached this state.

The real evil is the lack of alternatives sufficiently interesting to
make people want to do them instead of TV. Some of us get bored with
TV and seek other things. Others perhaps don't get above the
"action-reaction" mental state, and stay dependent on TV for
stimulation.

I'll agree with one thing Clinton said in his address last week: "We
need to give kids more things to say 'yes' to."

Sean

From sfraves-owner  Mon Jan 31 09:41:17 1994

Get your friends to go. Tell the promoters you came because you like

of people decide which event to go to by word of mouth.

Sean

From sfraves-owner  Mon Jan 31 09:53:00 1994
From: csc@pilot.njin.net (Sean Casey)
Date: Mon, 31 Jan 94 12:52:52 EST
Subject: Why I don't own a TV
Status: 

That was my revelation too when I stopped watching TV, how much time
it was using. At first, I didn't know what to do with the time. I'd
get bored. But after a while, other interests kick in, and the time is
filled doing other things.

I've got so many projects planned and going on now, I feel like I have
no time at all :). I wish there were more hours in the day. Boredom? I
let loose of that concept years and years ago. Nowdays when I find or
plan a few hours to just mope around, I simply relax and enjoy letting
the brain run on idle for a while.

My life is so much richer, because I get real intellectual and
creative stimulation from the things I do with my time. I can hardly
watch TV now; it feels like someone is injecting Novacaine into my
brain, a sensation I distinctly do not like.

Sean

Actually the TV itself is pretty innocuous (sp?).  In fact you are reading
this on a television of sorts (how much different *really* is a computer
monitor?).  It's like any other tool.  It's all in how you utilize it.  You

Re: TV

programming :-)

For ME,  a good TV program is NATURE,  or some first run nature program
like what might be on the Discovery channel.   I also like "Invention"
"Next Step",  and "Beyond 2000" as some of my favorites.  Very 
informative, some lame,  but all not bad.

Re: pirate radio...

>Well, it was great while it lasted. I got a tape (dunno how good it is) 
>from about 10:15 on. The evil five were just that, but my tape ran out on 
>evil number three.

WOW!  I would like to hear that tape.   I normally don't go on the netz
on Sundays,  so missed the news about the pirate station.   I used to
operate one myself in the 60's.    I remember Trickey Dick Nixon banning
certain songs because they had reference to drugs,   so we all went out
and aquired those songs,  and went on the air from my VW Bus.   I had
two battery operated turntables,  an FM transmitter that COULD put out
about 65 watts (but we didn't have set that powerful).    We went up into
the mountains over San Jose and was heard all over the bay area.
We called it "Free Wireless" or "San Jose Free Radio".   We learned
the FCC knew about it,  but we weren't sending out a "dirty" signal,
nor did we use profanity.    We used a phone company "Loop around" to
recieve phone calls from listeners.

If I remember,  I think I know the dude in Berkeley who is doing it,
but I doubt if he has the balls to go on the eir like this,  cause the
FCC already has a case pending on his previous activities.   Enough people
have bought his FM transmitter kits now,  so ANYONE could be doing it.
One can build a reasonably powerful FM transmitter for as little as $15
in parts.   Anyone having a pair od SL-1200's and a mixer could go on
the air.    Antennas can easily be constructed for less than $10.

The FCC has spectrum monitors,  usually in the hills in Livermore,   but
I'm sure they cover the bay area.    If a loud enough signel appears
on the FM band,   it signals attention.   Once the engineers detect
a signal,  they look for additional harmonics (Which are usually present
from hand made transmitters).   If they are interfering with other services,
they will attempt to locate it (Takes several seconds),  and go out and
investigate.   If not,   they usually will monitor it,   but most likeley
will not bother to shut it down.    Most people won't be going on the
air 12 hours a day for this very reason.    The FCC knows this,  and
has better things to do.   Don't ask me how I know all this,   but reliable
sources have enlightened me on this subject.
CC

Weekend Rave Report
===================

This weekend,  had to entertain some guests from Japan,   but they were
more then willing to also partake in a little bit of raving...

Late this Saturday afternoon,   We went to the Organic party in GG park.
WOW!!  What clean good energy...  It was bigger and better than I expected,
and almost immediately saw all of my friends.    My Japanese guests were
really digging it and daning around.    Really good afternoon trancey
music that turned on my luv phasers to full blast.    The afternoon was
warm and sunny and just perfect for dancing.

It was held between the Steinhart Aquerium and the musium.    A picture
perfect setting to have a rave.    I sure hope we can have more of these
in the future.    

Then,  we headed down the penninsula to go to "House Sweet House" on the
Stanford campus.    Mostly a college affair,   but the SF Ravers were
behind the turn tables that kept the place hopping with some really fine
"housey" sounds.   There was barely enough room to dance,  which was 
unfortunate,  because the music was totally insane.    Amit (one of our
local SFRavers had his MIDI system in the chill area,  pumping out
some awsome trancey stuff.

We left around 1:30 Am and headed back into SF to ceck out Kulture Shok
It was in a new warehouse,  very big high cielings,   and very ideal for 
dancing.    It was so cool that I was able to dance for hours without 
stopping.   Great Funkey music,  and all the usual DJ's were there.    
They had a super powerful sound system,  but the corrugated aluminum 
walls and ceiling didn't help much with the acoustics.    Apparently 
they had the base speakers pointing out AWAY from the center of the dance 
area,  and the higher treble sounds were focused towards dance area.     
There were some interesting acoustical effects while I was moving around 
the dance floor.   I would say about 350 raver folks showed up at this
one.    Unfortunately,  the $20 admittance fees kept a lot of the old
schoolers away from this one.

The crowd at "Kulture Shok" was rather young (It was an ALL AGES event).
Very early on,  people were starting to get all hosed out.    The dance
floor was getting rather empty,   and you can tell that acid must be
scarce these days.

After Kulture Shok,   headed out for breakfast then headed over to
the DNA.    Things weren't happening over there yet,  so headed over
to Atlantis.    Later, returned to the DNA but there wasn't much 
happening so it closed early.    That afternoon,  the weather was really
great,  and enjoyed the outside patio area at the End Up and danced till
about 4 pm.     They had some really good funkey house playing all 
afternoon.

Keep the ecene alive...
CC

they can't stop eating them.  Toad tells Frog that what they need to help them
stop eating the cookies is something called "willpower" and proceeds to put
the lid on the box of cookies.  Frog quickly points out that all they need
to do is take the lid off the box and they will be in the same predicament
so Toad then wraps the lid on with a ribbon.  Frog points out that the scissorsare only a few steps away and that they can cut the ribbon so Toad puts the
cookies on a high shelf.  Frog points out that they can use the same ladder
to get the cookies down and then use the scissors to cut the ribbon and  then
take the lid off the box to eat cookies again.  Finnaly, they throw the cookiesout the window and shout "Hey birds, here are cookies" and a hungry mob quicklyeats all the cookies.  Satisfyed,  they look at each other and agree that

From sfraves-owner  Mon Jan 31 20:05:38 1994
From: grigsby@agames.com (JUMP IN THE FIRE)
Date: Mon, 31 Jan 1994 20:04:08 -0800 (PST)
Subject: TV or not TV
Status: 


Jon said:

> > how about "control yourself"?  too many people these days are just
> > unwilling to take responsibility for their own actions.  this is why
> > things like the PMRC get anywhere, rather than being shot down in
> > hails of derisive laughter like they would in any sane society.

I will not force anyone to stop watching the tube.  I will point out
that my life improved drastically when I stopped.  We 100% agree here.

Someone else said:  (my mailer doesn't autoquote):

> Definantly true.  My problem with television, despite my jokes about
> missing not having one, isn't at all that I waste my time on it.  It's
> that it's such a valuable communications resource being badly misused,

This is the central myth of mass-media, which is that it is a neutral
medium and can be put to good use if we just figured out how.  Television,
magazines, newspapers, radio:  all give a very few the power to control
information flow to the many.

Remember the saying "Power corrupts, and absolute power corrupts
absolutely"?  For every Whole Earth Review sold there are tens of
thousands of Reader's Digests.  For every public access cable viewer
there are millions of people watching "Cops".  For every KPFK listener
there are millions of people dittoing Rush Limbaugh.

A distillation of the above two points: mass media is inherently fascistic.

I don't trust it one bit.  Any time I see a TV on in a house,
everyone's attention zips over to it and they stop interacting --
including me.  TV has been optimized for decades to catch your
attention with images and jingles, and it works.  You can't help but
be distracted by something that well-researched, especially when the
noise pervades the whole house.

I mean, sure, I _could_ leave a big sugar bowl full of blow on the
breakfast table, and if I had enough willpower I wouldn't have to
snort it all the time, but why waste the time tempting myself with
something I know I really don't want?

Kicking television is just like kicking any other drug.  If you want
to get off you don't surround yourself with it.  Now that I've been
off I can deal with my housemate's occasional laserdisc movies, but I
don't like them, and plan on finding TV-less housemates next time.

// g

From sfraves-owner  Mon Jan 31 20:19:40 1994
From: erikn@goldfish.mitron.tek.com (Erik Nilsson)
Date: Mon, 31 Jan 94 20:05:13 -0800
Subject: Re: NBC poll and other ings 
Status: 


This thread may be in danger of straying from raves ( :-)) but ...

Niels P. Mayer sez:

> with a 1-900 or 1-800 number, the people on the other end
> can easily get your phone #, and from that determine the caller, and
> from that they can probably correlate your vote to the number you are
> calling from, and perhaps to the caller him/her-self.

Technically, the 800- and 900-prefix information is called ANI, and is
passed from the Local Exchange Carrier to the long distance carrier
for billing purposes.  The long-distance carrier then passes it along
to the 800 or 900 subscriber in real-time, if the subscriber pays an
extra fee for this service.  The phone numbers always appear on the
(electronic) billing detail.  Phone number databases are then used to
determine billing information, as well probably income, probable
ethnicity, and so on.

The thing to remember is that ANI bears no relation to the information
used for Caller-ID, which is called CNI.  In some states, CNI can be
blocked from Caller-ID and similar services (often called CLASS
services), but ANI can never be blocked, because it is required for
long-distance billing, and there is no protocol for keeping the
long-distance carriers from giving the ANI information to anyone they
want.  The only way to block ANI is to launder your call through
something like "Private Lines" in Los Angeles.  Otherwise, 800 and 900
lines always know who you are (or who's phone you are calling from).

AmEx started using ANI, and for awhile they were greeting their
customers by name, as their account records come up.  Customers found
this very unnerving, and wasted a lot of AmEx's time with questions
about how AmEx knew customer names before customers announced
themselves.  So AmEx stopped greeting customers by name.  Of course,
they still use ANI to pull up customer records faster.

Another point which applies to both CNI and ANI: your number appears
even if you have a nunpublished/unlisted number.  For CNI, the billing
name is also delivered.

jamiel sez:

> I think the data (which I am sure is being collected) is more likely for
> marketing.

Yup.  Ever see one of those ads for a free 800-prefix phone call to
tell you today's pollen count in your area?  Those folx built a pretty
nice list of decongestant/antihistamine customers.


Chris Hart sez:

> I believe the media is using them to *calibrate* how well they are
> manipulating public opinion

I don't really know, but it seems unlikely that the media much cares
what public opinion is, except that dramatic or wierd swings in public
opinion are cheap sources of "news".  Advertisers care with all their
being about the public's opinion of their products.  Money is power,
follow the money.

- Erik

From sfraves-owner  Tue Feb  1 02:25:44 1994
From: mcg@netcom.com (MykL G)
Date: Tue, 1 Feb 1994 02:31:14 -0800
Subject: Re: NBC poll (and is TV really evil)?
Status: 

> Chris Hart sez:
> 
> > I believe the media is using them to *calibrate* how well they are
> > manipulating public opinion

Actually, it was I who said that, not Chris Hart...  =)

> I don't really know, but it seems unlikely that the media much cares
> what public opinion is, except that dramatic or wierd swings in public
> opinion are cheap sources of "news".  Advertisers care with all their
> being about the public's opinion of their products.  Money is power,
> follow the money.

You may be right that the media doesn't really care what the public
really think, at least about some issues.  But on issues like foreign
policy (not a *single* media commentator so much as questioned our "right"
to invade Panama, capture their head of state and bomb thousands of innocent
civilians to death in the process) or health care (single payer system?
That would cripple one of our biggest segments of advertisers) the media
constantly marginalizes perfectly reasonable views and pushes them
off of the scale of "acceptable" opinion.  Look how they treat drug
decriminalization.  Look what they did to Loni Guinier, or even Robert
Bork (yes, it can operate on both ends of the spectrum, but far more
often to one particular side.)

And even if I can't convince you of that (and I think Chomsky would
if you read him or see the movie), maintaining their *ability* to
manipulate public opinion is crucial, and these 900 feedback polls
are the ideal way to perfect their technique.  After all, they are merely
polls of people who are consumers of their particular medium, "not a
scientific sampling of the population", although the poll results strongly
influence those whose views are less certain.  They need to maintain this
ability in order to exert their power over politicians of either party.
(And the fact that they also produce revenue is icing on the cake!)

Advertisers care about far more than the popularity of their products.
Even more important is not to upset the status quo that lets them
operate under social conditions that if enough people started thinking
for themselves and demanding change, could be altered significantly to
their disadvantage.

Another strong point Chomsky makes is that built-in to the format
of those ubiquitous news-analysis programs (Nightline, Crossfire, MacNeil/
Lehrer, etc.) guest "experts" are only allowed 30 seconds or a minute at a
time to make their point, which means that anyone with an unorthodox
viewpoint would never have time to explain their position.  That's why
the guests on these programs always represent positions that are within
the boundaries of the "national party line", largely shared by both parties.

The media set the agenda, so that the people themselves cannot.  That's why
the percentage of people who rate crime as the #1 problem has more than
quadrupled since the clearly contrived over-exploitation of the Polly Klaas
story.  It surprises me that that isn't obvious to everyone.  (JSLee: 
please tell us you *were* being sarcastic about Hard Copy, Inside Edition,
etc. yes?)

Aside from Chomsky, two other excellent critiques of television I recommend
are _Amusing Ourselves to Death_ by Neil Postman, and _Four Arguments
for the Elimination of Television_ by Jerry Mander.  Postman basically
describes how TV has structurally reduced the terms of America's political
and social debate to the most trivial lowest common denominator.  Mander 
(a former advertising executive) makes many cogent observations; one I 
happen to like has to do with the hypnotic, addictive trance-like state
that is induced by the CRT scan (but there are dozens of other equally
powerful arguments).

I lived happily without TV for periods of my life, but I'll admit that
there are occasionally things worth watching (like Tales of the City!)
Still, I think we have an obligation to understand and challenge the
iron grip it has on the mentality of our society.

This is also why it's crucial that we demand the many-to-many model of the
Internet prevail in the current "Information Superhighway" debate -- if
the cable TV people win out on this one, we're all in *deep* shit folx.
500 channels deep!

- MykL G

"Propaganda is to a democracy what violence is to a dictatorship."
- N. Chomsky



(OK, Brian, are you going to take the list down again now...?  =)

From sfraves-owner  Tue Feb  1 10:16:59 1994

This is pretty inaccurate.  It's pretty hard to "overdose" on E in one
shot (I've read reports of effects at >1000mg, and while it wasn't
dangerous per se, it was a bit overwhelming.)  The ratio of lethal
dose to active dose is much larger than you suggest (I don't think a
lethal dose even HAS been determined for E - I'd bet it's comparable
to the lethal dose of cauliflower :) What you may be thinking of is
the self-anti-addictive nature of E over the long run - the fact that
people generally do not abuse the drug, because the more often it's
taken, the lesser the effects.  Right now the only medical concern is
that when someone takes E for the first time, it (I can't remember
correctly) either depletea the brain of serotonin, or causes the brain
to stop the production of serotonin.  This comes back 18 months later,
supposedly.  The big question is - does this make any difference? 

Anyways, for more complete references, check out "Ecstasy - the MDMA
story" by Bruce Eisner - I think it just went into its second
printing, because I saw it had a new cover yesterday while in the
bookstore.  There are other good books too, like the above-mentioned
Psychedelic Encyclopedia by Peter Stafford.  Also, I've archived a ton
of stuff about E on the FTP/gopher/www site on techno.stanford.edu,
under /pub/raves/chemistry/mdma.  Check it out.

Finally, whilst on the topic of books.... while looking for a book
yesterday at Cody's I decided to check out one of the many books on
the Internet, and found a listing for sfraves in two of the three I
picked up!  They even had the correct address :)

From sfraves-owner  Tue Feb  1 10:46:46 1994
From: ccat@netcom.com (Chris Beaumont)
Date: Tue, 1 Feb 1994 10:47:14 -0800
Subject: Degeneration of Serotonergic Neurons and MDMA toxicity..
Status: 

   Ann started a thread that basically contains what I can gather about
the current opinion of the toxicity of MDMA.Basically,the studies
indicating serotonin receptor damage and a decrease in serotonin (5HT)
levels were derived from a study that used huge,intervenous doses of MDMA,
a pattern not at all similar to the usual vectors of human ingestion
(oral,much lower dose/kg) Note that I'm not absolutely sure of the gist
of this citation,since its something I overheard at a potluck last
Friday,but basically this is the current situation as I see it.

A more recent study,also using monkeys,found no measurable serotonin terminal
degeneration with similar doses /kg as are typical in human use patterns
and found no measurable brain damage.However,I would caution against the 
use of street drugs of unknown origin.I think further research needs to be
encouraged in "safe" intoxicants,since people like to alter their states 
of mind.This needs to be done by reputable laboratories.Education and risk 
reduction should be the primary method of drug 
control.Perhaps a distinction might be made between synthetic and natural
substances and synthetics be regulated through some kind of licensing
system so they could remain to be used in psychotherapy and guided 
consciousness exploration.Natural entheogens with a history of legitimate
religious use could be exempted unless their use began to pose social 
problems.This would enable a sense of reconciliation between the generational
factions and would empower a new truth and community based pragmatic
approach to the drug problem. Misinformation campaigns sour the atmosphere
...


From sfraves-owner  Tue Feb  1 10:51:07 1994
From: Robert Jesse <rjesse@us.oracle.com>
Date: Tue, 1 Feb 94 10:41:08 -0800
Subject: Re: MDMA & neurons
Status: 

>From: anthrax@uclink.berkeley.edu (Ann Elizabeth Loraine)
>
>I was talking to a somebody Sat. who'd been to a lecture on the effects
>of E on your serotinergic neurons.  The effect is that E in large doses
>kills off these neurons' connections, but that over time, they grow back
>in _different places_.  Has anybody heard of this result?  I'd love to
>hear more and if you can tell me a source, please do.

you may be referring to a primate study (unpublished) conducted by george  
ricaurte.  in dosing primates with MDMA and then looking for morphological  
changes in their brains, he found that there was no effect after 2.5 mg/kg  
doses given every two weeks, for a total of eight doses.  but after a single  
oral dose of 5 mg/kg, he observed a 20% reduction in serotonergic neurons,  
only in the thalamus & hypothalamus.  there appears to be some regrowth over  
time, not necessarily complete, and also some "collateral sprouting" - growth  
of other types of neurons in the reduced serotonin areas.

note that in all of the animal studies, even when there are quite large  
serotonin system reductions (up to 90% in high MDMA dose rat studies), no  
behavioral deficits are observed.

it is also uncertain how these studies would extrapolate to humans - the  
human brain may well be more or less sensitive, or sensitive in different  
areas, compared with other animals.  in any case, what is known is that there  
are no reported cases that link behavior changes in humans with MDMA-induced  
serotonin system changes or "damage".   and, the long-term human behavior  
changes that are noted (in studies and from anecdotal case reports) are  
generally regarded as positive - lowered impulsiveness and hostility,  
improved social/interpersonal functioning, changes in religious/spiritual  
orientation or practice, etc.

one of the reasons so little is known about the physical effects of MDMA on  
the human brain is that no subjects (to date) have recorded their drug use  
history, then volunteered their brains for study.  if you would like to  
consider doing this, you can get donor information at 1-800-UM-BRAIN.

---------------------------MicrosoftNews-------------------------------
 
!1 OTC  04/1 0947  MICROSOFT ANNOUNCES BETA RELEASE OF WINDOWS TP
 
REDMOND, WA (JAN. 13) BUSINESS WIRE - Microsoft Corp. announced
Thursday that a beta release of Windows TP, the telepathic operating
system, was released to 1,500 test sites worldwide.
   Developed using the soon-to-be released Microsoft C for Neurons,
Windows TP bypasses awkward user interfaces by interacting directly
with the user's brain. Using the Microsoft MindMouse, users can
visualize images in their mind, and the application associated with
that image (or "thought icon") is executed. Users can visualize
pictures to create Windows Bitmap images, or think text directly into
Windows applications. Windows TP is fully compatible with all previous
versions of Windows.
   Data stored under Windows TP can be copied into the user's
short-term memory (the Windows TP Clipboard), or transferred
directly into the user's long-term memory using Windows' new 32-bit
Direct Neuron Access technology.  Users can then plug into other
Windows TP systems to transfer the data.
   Microsoft also announced the first application developed exclusively
for Windows TP. CyberMail is a mental mail system designed to
transfer messages by thought. Users visualize the person or company
logo they want to send a message to, followed by the message to send.
Microsoft has had a beta version of the application in use for several
months.
   Founded in 1975, Microsoft (NASDAQ:MSFT) is the worldwide leader in
software for personal computers. he company offers a wide range of
products and services for business and personal use, each designed with
the mission of making it easier and more enjoyable for people to take
advantage of the full power of personal computing every day.
 
CONTACT: Microsoft Corporation Liz Wagthor, 206/555-8080 (CyberMail
address:  A short, dumpy lady, with shiny red hair, and a really gross
mole growing on the right side of her lip.  A blue tattoo on her right
arm says, "Billy G's the Man for Me.")
 
!2 RTf  04/01 1012  TESTERS REPORT PROBLEMS WITH WINDOWS TP BETA
 
   NEW YORK, Apr 1, Reuter - Microsoft's new Windows TP has a long way
to go before final release, say beta testers of the product.
   Testers report numerous problems with the thought icons included
with the product.
   "I can see a fish tail representing some useful things, but the
Program Manager? It's just not intuitive," says Clyde Revlon, an MIS
specialist with McBalmy, Crain, and Larch. "Whoever came up with these
thought icons needs therapy. I'm sure the guy's Yorkshire terrier is
wonderful, but as the File Manager? A golden retriever I could
understand. And that sweater the terrier is wearing, it's just too
loud. Let me control the sweater."
   Testers also report dangerous corruption problems with the Direct
Neuron Access technology. "Colors, I smell colors. Dog, good dog, go
to the light mom," said Maggie Ferreaux, a consultant with Sharp,
Trenchant, and Blunt Computer Services.
   Other testers were less understanding. "I'm working on a
presentation, and suddenly all I can think about is pages A through
C of the Miami telephone directory. It took me three hours to get it
out of my mind. That blows my productivity right out of the water,"
says Max Pirenich, a salesman for Carp Technology. "Just thinking about
Excel scares the crap out of me."
   Microsoft officials acknowledged the issues, citing that no beta
release of a product is perfect, and vowed to provide testers with
the services of the same Neurologist that helped Microsoft Quality
Assurance recover from testing the product in its early stages. Many
Microsoft QA engineers are expected to lead long, productive lives.
 REUTER

From sfraves-owner  Tue Feb  1 11:39:33 1994

On Tue, 1 Feb 1994, Greg Beckham wrote:

> hmmm... well from what ive read, the effects of pure MDMA are not
> heightened by taking larger and larger doses- they quickly become
> dangerous (large doses), because the overdose threshold (or margin) is so
> small- the difference between a good dose and a 'overdose' is only about
> 100 to 125 mg.... since i dont have my source right in front of me- ill
> post the information as soon as i can get to the book... (the Psychedelic
> Encyclopedia, which is by far, not a medical source, but is edited for
> accuracy by a doctor....)
> 
> peace out!
> greg
> 

Barring any previous medical condition to which stimulants are a risk, 
larger and larger doses are not so much *dangerous* as they are 
*unpleasant*. After two or so, the stimulant effect starts to overshadow 
the enactogenic effects. Hard to feel much empathy when your teeth are 
clattering and your sweating bullets. That's why it isn't considered an 
addictive drug. The more you take, the worse it gets. The fine line is 
between enactogen and stimulant. It varies from person to person but 
usually ends up around the dose you mentioned above. Anything higher and 
you usually just end up chewing a hole through your cheek <grin>. JMAR.

From sfraves-owner  Tue Feb  1 12:16:57 1994
From: Tanya Smith <tmsmith@gibbs.oit.unc.edu>

On Tue, 1 Feb 1994, Robert Jesse wrote:

> Date: Tue, 1 Feb 94 10:41:08 -0800
> From: Robert Jesse <rjesse@us.oracle.com>
> To: sfraves@techno.Stanford.EDU
> Subject: Re: MDMA & neurons
> 
> >From: anthrax@uclink.berkeley.edu (Ann Elizabeth Loraine)
> >
> >I was talking to a somebody Sat. who'd been to a lecture on the effects
> >of E on your serotinergic neurons.  The effect is that E in large doses
> >kills off these neurons' connections, but that over time, they grow back
> >in _different places_.  Has anybody heard of this result?  I'd love to
> >hear more and if you can tell me a source, please do.
> 
> you may be referring to a primate study (unpublished) conducted by george  
> ricaurte.  in dosing primates with MDMA and then looking for morphological  
> changes in their brains, he found that there was no effect after 2.5 mg/kg  
> doses given every two weeks, for a total of eight doses.  but after a single  
> oral dose of 5 mg/kg, he observed a 20% reduction in serotonergic neurons,  
> only in the thalamus & hypothalamus.  there appears to be some regrowth over  
> time, not necessarily complete, and also some "collateral sprouting" - growth  
> of other types of neurons in the reduced serotonin areas.

In case you people don't know, low serotonin levels are responsible for
depression, obsessive-compulsive behavior, suicide, eating disorders, etc.
 Violent behavior is also a result from screwing with these fragile
receptors.  So look before you leap.  If you already have low serotonin
levels, prolonged use of extasy could exacerbate your problem.
Finally, ANY drug can be psychologically addictive.  Beware how casual you
treat this facet of addiction because psychological addiction can be just
as powerful as physical addiction.
TMS


> 
> note that in all of the animal studies, even when there are quite large  
> serotonin system reductions (up to 90% in high MDMA dose rat studies), no  
> behavioral deficits are observed.
> 
> it is also uncertain how these studies would extrapolate to humans - the  
> human brain may well be more or less sensitive, or sensitive in different  
> areas, compared with other animals.  in any case, what is known is that there  
> are no reported cases that link behavior changes in humans with MDMA-induced  
> serotonin system changes or "damage".   and, the long-term human behavior  
> changes that are noted (in studies and from anecdotal case reports) are  
> generally regarded as positive - lowered impulsiveness and hostility,  
> improved social/interpersonal functioning, changes in religious/spiritual  
> orientation or practice, etc.
> 
> one of the reasons so little is known about the physical effects of MDMA on  
> the human brain is that no subjects (to date) have recorded their drug use  
> history, then volunteered their brains for study.  if you would like to  
> consider doing this, you can get donor information at 1-800-UM-BRAIN.
